Nasafi's Distinction: Committer of Major Sin and Kufr
Nasafi defines the committer of major sin and distinguishes kufr from other sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's distinction between a committer of a major sin who remains a believer and one who becomes a disbeliever.

Core Idea
Only deeming sin permissible or belittling Shari'a makes a grave sinner a disbeliever.

Nasafi begins by clarifying the status of those who commit major sins. He wants to avoid the extremes of the Khawarij (who declare all grave sinners disbelievers) and the Murji'a (who say sin does not affect faith). In this passage, he sets the criteria that preserve faith despite sin, and identifies when sin becomes kufr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key terms
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the terms 'mustahill' (deeming permissible) and 'mustahin' (belittling). These are central to the distinction.
The term 'mukhallad fi al-niran' (eternally in the Fire) is also important.
2
Understand the two conditions for kufr
Nasafi lists two reasons that turn a major sin into kufr: deeming it permissible (istihlal) and belittling the Shari'a (istihana). Without these, the person remains a believer.
This contrasts with the Khawarij who claim any major sin causes kufr.
3
Compare with the second distinction
Nasafi adds that kufr is a permanent creed, while other sins are temporary acts with intent to repent. This reinforces that a sinner who intends to repent is not a kafir.
The resolve to repent (ʿazm ʿala al-tawba) is key.
4
Summarize Nasafi's position
Now write a brief summary: a Muslim who commits a major sin without istihlal or istihana remains a believer, but if either condition exists, he becomes a kafir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي أن مرتكب الكبيرة المخلد في النيران هو المستحل لها والمستهين بأمور الشرع. ويفرق النسفي بين الكفر وبين ما ارتكب من الذنوب الأخرى من ذلك: أولاً: من عصى الله بنوع من الكبائر غير مستحل لها ولا مستهين بالشرع فهو مؤمن وما كان مستحلا ومستهينا فهو كافر لأنه منكر مكذب. ثانياً: أن الكفر مذهب يعتقد للأبد أما غيره فهو مرتبط بوقت الكبيرة وهو غلبة الشهوة لا للأبد وفي عقيدته العزم على التوبة. والنسفي يكفر الخوارج والمعتزلة لقولهم في مرتكب الكبيرة، مستدلا على ذلك بأنهم يئسوا من رحمة الله، وقد قال تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَا يَأْيَنَسُ مِن رَوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ [يوسف: 87] ويدير حول كلامه هذا نقاشا عقليا ليؤكد رأيه في كفر المعتزلة والخوارج.
And Nasafi mentions that the one who commits a major sin who is eternally in the Fire is the one who deems it permissible and belittles the matters of the Shari'a. And Nasafi distinguishes between kufr and other committed sins: firstly, whoever disobeys God with a type of major sin without deeming it permissible nor belittling the Shari'a is a believer, and whoever deems it permissible and belittles is a disbeliever because he is a denier and a falsifier. Secondly, kufr is a sect believed forever, whereas other sins are tied to the time of the major sin and are a dominance of desire, not forever, and in his belief is the resolve to repent. And Nasafi declares as disbelievers the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila because of their statement regarding the committer of major sin, arguing that they have despaired of God's mercy, and He, the Most High, said: 'Indeed, none despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people' [Yusuf: 87]. And he conducts around this speech a rational discussion to confirm his view regarding the disbelief of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's position on the committer of a major sin (kabira). He begins by stating that the one who commits a major sin and is eternally in the Fire is only the one who deems the sin permissible (mustahill) or belittles the matters of the Shari'a (mustahin). Then he provides a twofold distinction between kufr and other sins: First, a person who commits a major sin without deeming it permissible or belittling the Shari'a remains a believer. Only when he deems it permissible or belittles does he become a kafir, because that constitutes denial (inkar) and falsification (takdhib). Second, kufr is a persistent creed (madhhab) held forever, whereas other sins are temporary acts arising from overpowering desire, and the sinner intends to repent. On this basis, Nasafi declares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila to be disbelievers because they rule that a committer of major sin is a kafir, which implies they have despaired of God's mercy, contrary to the Quranic verse. He then indicates that he provides rational argumentation to support his takfir of these groups.

Key Terms
مرتكب الكبيرة
مستحل
مستهين
كفر
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any major sin makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that only when the sin is deemed permissible (istihlal) or the Shari'a is belittled (istihana) does it become kufr.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what makes a committer of a major sin a disbeliever?
A) Simply committing the major sin
B) Deeming the sin permissible or belittling the Shari'a
C) Despairing of God's mercy
D) Not repenting immediately

Key Takeaway
A major sinner remains a believer unless he deems the sin permissible or belittles the Shari'a; kufr is a permanent creed, not a temporary act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin while secretly telling yourself it's not that bad or that Allah will surely forgive you? If that thought crosses your mind, are you dangerously close to istihlal? What will you do today to guard against justifying your sins?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, do your thoughts immediately move to judgment and condemnation? Nasafi's distinction forces you to check your own heart: are you labeling them a disbeliever without proof? What does that say about your own faith and fear of Allah?
?
You have major sins in your past—some repeated, some not yet repented from. Do you truly have the resolve to repent (al-azm 'ala al-tawba) that Nasafi says is part of a believer's belief? If not, what is stopping you from making that resolve today, right now?

Nasafi's Takfir of Khawarij and Mu'tazila
Nasafi declares takfir of Khawarij and Mu'tazila for their view on major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi declares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila as disbelievers based on their view of the committer of major sin.

Core Idea
Declaring that a grave sinner is a kafir leads to despair of God's mercy, which is kufr.

After establishing his own position, Nasafi turns to refute the Khawarij and Mu'tazila. These groups held that the committer of a major sin is a kafir (Khawarij) or in a state between faith and disbelief (Mu'tazila). Nasafi argues that their ruling implies despair of God's mercy, which contradicts the Quran and constitutes kufr.

Guided Path
1
Read Nasafi's claim and the Quranic verse
Read the Arabic: Nasafi declares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila as disbelievers and cites Quran 12:87. Understand the reasoning.
The verse says none despairs of Allah's mercy except the disbelieving people.
2
Analyze the logical connection
By ruling that a grave sinner is a kafir, these groups are effectively saying that such a person has no hope of mercy, which is despair. Despair itself is a sign of disbelief.
This is the core of Nasafi's argument.
3
Consider the rational discussion mentioned
Nasafi mentions that he conducts a rational discussion (niqash ʿaqli) to support his view. Think about what such arguments might involve.
This likely includes logical proofs that the Mu'tazili position leads to contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implications
Nasafi's takfir of these groups shows the seriousness of theological errors. It highlights the Maturidi concern for mercy and the balance between hope and fear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر النسفي أن مرتكب الكبيرة المخلد في النيران هو المستحل لها والمستهين بأمور الشرع. ويفرق النسفي بين الكفر وبين ما ارتكب من الذنوب الأخرى من ذلك: أولاً: من عصى الله بنوع من الكبائر غير مستحل لها ولا مستهين بالشرع فهو مؤمن وما كان مستحلا ومستهينا فهو كافر لأنه منكر مكذب. ثانياً: أن الكفر مذهب يعتقد للأبد أما غيره فهو مرتبط بوقت الكبيرة وهو غلبة الشهوة لا للأبد وفي عقيدته العزم على التوبة. والنسفي يكفر الخوارج والمعتزلة لقولهم في مرتكب الكبيرة، مستدلا على ذلك بأنهم يئسوا من رحمة الله، وقد قال تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّهُ لَا يَأْيَنَسُ مِن رَوْحِ اللَّهِ إِلَّا الْقَوْمُ الْكَافِرُونَ ﴾ [يوسف: 87] ويدير حول كلامه هذا نقاشا عقليا ليؤكد رأيه في كفر المعتزلة والخوارج.
And Nasafi mentions that the one who commits a major sin who is eternally in the Fire is the one who deems it permissible and belittles the matters of the Shari'a. And Nasafi distinguishes between kufr and other committed sins: firstly, whoever disobeys God with a type of major sin without deeming it permissible nor belittling the Shari'a is a believer, and whoever deems it permissible and belittles is a disbeliever because he is a denier and a falsifier. Secondly, kufr is a sect believed forever, whereas other sins are tied to the time of the major sin and are a dominance of desire, not forever, and in his belief is the resolve to repent. And Nasafi declares as disbelievers the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila because of their statement regarding the committer of major sin, arguing that they have despaired of God's mercy, and He, the Most High, said: 'Indeed, none despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people' [Yusuf: 87]. And he conducts around this speech a rational discussion to confirm his view regarding the disbelief of the Mu'tazila and the Khawarij.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's position on the committer of a major sin (kabira). He begins by stating that the one who commits a major sin and is eternally in the Fire is only the one who deems the sin permissible (mustahill) or belittles the matters of the Shari'a (mustahin). Then he provides a twofold distinction between kufr and other sins: First, a person who commits a major sin without deeming it permissible or belittling the Shari'a remains a believer. Only when he deems it permissible or belittles does he become a kafir, because that constitutes denial (inkar) and falsification (takdhib). Second, kufr is a persistent creed (madhhab) held forever, whereas other sins are temporary acts arising from overpowering desire, and the sinner intends to repent. On this basis, Nasafi declares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila to be disbelievers because they rule that a committer of major sin is a kafir, which implies they have despaired of God's mercy, contrary to the Quranic verse. He then indicates that he provides rational argumentation to support his takfir of these groups.

يأس من رحمة الله

Correction: Nasafi considers their view as leading to despair of mercy, which is a form of disbelief according to the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi declares the Khawarij and Mu'tazila disbelievers because their view on major sinners entails despair of God's mercy, which is kufr.

?
Nasafi declares takfir based on a theological belief that implicitly denies God's mercy. Examine your own beliefs: Do you hold any view that might inadvertently limit God's forgiveness? How can you align your heart with the Ahl al-Sunna position that God's mercy encompasses all sins for those who repent?

Definition of Iman According to Different Sects
Nasafi enumerates definitions of faith from various theological schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different definitions of faith (iman) proposed by major Islamic theological sects as reported by Nasafi.

Core Idea
Faith is defined differently by each sect—knowledge, confession, and action.

Nasafi begins his discussion of faith by presenting the views of various theological schools. Each sect defines iman differently, highlighting the core components: knowledge of the heart, verbal confession, and action. This passage introduces these key positions, setting the stage for deeper analysis of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get an overview of the definitions.
Notice how each definition is attributed to a specific group.
2
Identify each definition and its proponents
List the four definitions: knowledge+confession+action (Ahl al-Hadith), heart+tongue (Hanafiyya, Najjariyya), tongue only (Karramiyya), knowledge only (Jahmiyya).
Observe the widening spectrum from all-inclusive to minimal.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these differences matter for theology and practice. Does faith include actions?
This connects to broader debates on faith and works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هو الإيمان؟ الكلام في الإيمان. يبدأ النسفي إجابة عن هذا السؤال بعرض مجموعة من تعريفات للإيمان قالت بها الفرق الكلامية ناسبا كل تعريف إلى صاحبه. من ذلك القول بأن الإيمان معرفة بالقلب وإقرار باللسان وعمل بالأركان وينسب هذا القول إلى أصحاب الحديث. ومنهم من قال إن الإيمان يكون بالقلب واللسان وينسب هذا القول إلى كثير من طوائف المتكلمين، وعلى رأسهم الحنفية، والنجارية. أما الكرامية وعبد الله بن سعيد وغيرهما فإنهم يقولون: إن الإيمان يكون باللسان فقط. وهناك طائفة الجهمية وغيرهم يقولون: إن الإيمان هو المعرفة.
What is faith? Discussion on faith. Nasafi begins an answer to this question by presenting a set of definitions of faith that the theological sects have said, attributing each definition to its proponent. Among that is the statement that faith is knowledge by the heart, affirmation by the tongue, and action by the limbs, and he attributes this statement to the Ahl al-Hadith. And among them is he who said that faith is by the heart and the tongue, and he attributes this statement to many of the groups of theologians, foremost among them the Hanafiyya and the Najjariyya. As for the Karramiyya, 'Abdullah ibn Sa'id, and others, they say that faith is by the tongue only. And there is the sect of the Jahmiyya and others who say that faith is knowledge.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage lists the various definitions of iman (faith) according to different Islamic theological schools, as reported by Nasafi. The first definition is from Ahl al-Hadith: faith consists of knowledge in the heart, verbal confession, and action with the limbs. The second, attributed to many mutakallimun (theologians) including the Hanafiyya and Najjariyya, holds that faith is only in the heart and tongue, excluding actions. The third, from the Karramiyya and Abdallah ibn Sa'id, defines faith as purely verbal confession. The fourth, from the Jahmiyya, defines faith as mere knowledge. Thus, the schools differ on whether actions and verbal confession are part of the essence of faith.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think there is a single definition of iman in Islam.
Correction: Nasafi shows that various sects have different definitions, highlighting theological diversity.

Checkpoint

B) Hanafiyya

Key Takeaway
The definition of iman varies among Islamic sects, ranging from a three-part concept including action to a minimal definition as mere knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage reveals deep disagreement among Muslims on the essence of faith. How does that challenge your assumption that there is one simple 'Islamic' answer? Are you willing to study these differences humbly, or do you dismiss them as irrelevant?

Iman as Tasdiq: The Linguistic and Scholarly Foundation
Defines iman as tasdiq with linguistic and Quranic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of iman as affirmation (tasdiq) of the heart, its linguistic basis, and its distinction from actions.

Core Idea
Iman is affirmation by the heart, not a combination of belief and works.

This lesson examines the foundational definition of iman (faith) as tasdiq (affirmation) by the heart, a view attributed to major Sunni authorities including Abu Hanifa, al-Maturidi, and al-Ash'ari. The author presents linguistic evidence from Arabic usage and a Quranic verse to demonstrate that iman is distinct from righteous deeds. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping subsequent theological discussions on faith and works.

Guided Path
1
Read the scholarly positions
Identify the authorities cited who defined iman as tasdiq and note that al-Nasafi chose this view.
These are early Sunni imams, showing agreement across schools.
2
Understand the linguistic proof
The passage states that in the Arabic language, iman means tasdiq and its opposite is takdhib. Confirm this meaning in your mind.
Linguistic usage is a primary source for defining terms in kalam.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the verse from Surah Hud: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds.' Note how 'believed' (amanoo) is separate from 'done righteous deeds' (amal salih).
The conjunction 'and' (wa) indicates distinction, not identity.
4
Reflect on the implication
The conclusion is that iman is not the same as action. Consider why this distinction matters for theology and practice.
This definition impacts debates about sin, salvation, and the nature of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : إن الإيمان هو التصديق بالقلب، وهو مروي عن أبي حنيفة وأبي منصور الماتريدي، والحسين بن الفضل البجلي ، وأبي الحسن الأشعري. وقد اختار النسفي الرأي الأخير، وأخذ يقيم الأدلة على صحته. فالإيمان عند أهل اللغة التصديق، وضده التكذيب. والله سبحانه يقول : إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ ﴾ [هود: ۲۳] فالإيمان غير العمل.
And among them are those who said: Verily, faith (al-iman) is affirmation (al-tasdiq) by the heart, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifa, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, al-Husayn ibn al-Fadl al-Bajali, and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. And al-Nasafi chose the latter opinion, and he proceeded to establish proofs for its correctness. So faith, according to the people of language, is affirmation (al-tasdiq), and its opposite is denial (al-takdhib). And Allah the Exalted says: 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds' [Hud 11:23]; so faith is other than action.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of iman as tasdiq (affirmation) in the heart, citing early authorities including Abu Hanifa, al-Maturidi, and al-Ash'ari, and states that al-Nasafi adopted this view. It then provides the linguistic foundation: iman in Arabic means tasdiq, and its opposite is takdhib. The first proof is a Quranic verse that distinguishes iman from righteous deeds, showing they are separate concepts.

Correction: This passage clarifies that iman is solely affirmation (tasdiq) of the heart, and actions are separate, as shown by the Quranic verse.

Quranic Evidence for Iman Being Separate from Works
Cites Quranic verses showing iman includes sinners and precedes works

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three Quranic proofs that iman (faith) is distinct from actions and remains with believers even when they sin.

Core Idea
The Quran calls sinners believers and commands actions after faith — proving iman is separate.

This passage presents three key Quranic arguments used by Maturidi scholars to establish that iman (tasdiq) is distinct from works. By examining how the Quran addresses sinners as believers, how even enemies turn to tasdiq at the moment of truth, and how works are commanded after addressing believers, we see the clear separation between faith and deeds. This lays a foundation for the Maturidi position that faith does not increase or decrease with actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the three distinct proof citations.
2
Identify the three proofs
Locate each of the three Quranic arguments. Note which verses are cited and what point each makes.
Proof 1: sinners called believers. Proof 2: Pharaoh's tasdiq. Proof 3: fasting verse.
3
Reflect on the first proof
The Quran addresses those who commit murder (a major sin) as 'O you who have believed.' What does that tell us about their iman? It remains despite sin.
This counters the Kharijite view that major sins nullify iman.
4
Analyze the Pharaoh example
At the moment of drowning, Pharaoh says 'amantu' (I believe), not 'I did good works.' This shows that tasdiq alone is what matters for faith, not deeds.
Even an enemy turns to tasdiq when reality hits.
5
Consider the temporal order in the fasting verse
Allah says 'O you who have believed, fasting is prescribed...' — iman is addressed first, then the command. This shows iman precedes and is independent of the action.
If iman were the action itself, the address would be redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الله تعالى خاطب من ارتكب المعاصي الموجبة للحدود باسم المؤمنين في مثل قوله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۷۸] ويؤكد معنى التصديق أن الأعداء يلجأون إلى التصديق دون غيره عند معاينة العذاب نحو قول فرعون حينما أدركه الغرق: آمَنتُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا الَّذِى ءَامَنَتْ بِهِ بنوا إسرءيل ﴾ [يونس: ٩٠] أي صدقت، والله سبحانه وتعالى خاطب باسم الإيمان، ثم أوجب بعد ذلك الأعمال في نحو قوله تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۸۳].
Then indeed Allah the Exalted addressed those who committed sins necessitating legal punishments by the name of believers, such as in His statement: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution in cases of murder' [al-Baqarah 2:178]. And the meaning of affirmation is emphasized by the fact that the enemies resort to affirmation without anything else at the sight of punishment, like the statement of Pharaoh when drowning overtook him: 'I believe that there is no deity except that in which the Children of Israel believe' [Yunus 10:90], meaning 'I affirmed.' And Allah the Exalted addressed by the name of faith (al-iman), then He made actions obligatory after that, such as in His statement: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is fasting' [al-Baqarah 2:183].
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents two additional Quranic proofs that iman is distinct from works. First, Allah addresses sinners deserving of legal punishments as 'O you who have believed,' showing they retain iman despite sins. Second, the story of Pharaoh demonstrates that tasdiq alone is what matters at the moment of truth, not actions. Third, the verse about fasting commands works after addressing believers by their iman, proving iman precedes and is separate from the commanded works.

المؤمنين
المعاصي

Correction: The Quran addresses sinners who commit capital crimes as 'believers,' showing that iman remains with them despite sins.

Key Takeaway

Iman is Tasdiq Only: Logical and Hadith Proofs
Presents a logical reductio and hadith evidence that iman is only tasdiq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical and hadith arguments that demonstrate iman is exclusively tasdiq (affirmation of the heart) and does not include actions as constituent parts.

Core Idea
Iman is only tasdiq; actions are separate fruits, not parts.

This lesson presents a powerful logical reductio and two hadith proofs to establish that iman is nothing but tasdiq. The logical argument shows that if iman included actions, removing any action would destroy iman—contradicting the consensus that iman is affirmed by mere belief. The hadith evidence from Jibril’s question and from the listing of best deeds confirm that iman is distinct from actions. These arguments support the Maturidi/Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic source text once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the argumentative flow.
2
Understand the logical reductio
Analyze the first argument: if iman had three pillars, losing any one would lose iman, but Muslims accept iman by belief alone—contradiction.
3
Analyze the hadith of Jibril
See how the Prophet answered Jibril by listing only beliefs (tasdiq). If iman included actions, the answer would be incomplete and misleading.
4
Analyze the hadith on best deeds
Note that the Prophet listed 'iman' separately from 'jihad' and 'hajj', showing iman is not a deed.
5
Reflect on al-Nasafi's conclusion
Understand that al-Nasafi refutes opposing views to uphold the Sunni position that iman is only tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الإيمان إذا كان عبارة عن التصديق والإقرار والعمل فمعنى ذلك أنه إذا زال ركن من الأركان الثلاثة زال الإيمان مع أن المسلمين أثبتوا حكمه بمجرد الاعتقاد قبل وجود غيره من الأعمال. ثم إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حينما سأله جبريل عليه السلام عن الإيمان أجاب: "أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته.. إلخ" أي أن تصدق، ولو كان الإيمان غير التصديق لكان مجيء جبريل ليلبس على الناس لا ليعلمهم وكان النبي مقصرا في الجواب. وقول النبي حينما سئل عن أفضل الأعمال: "إيمان" لا شك فيه، وجهاد لا غلول فيه، وحج مبرور ففي كلامه عليه السلام مغايرة بين الإيمان، والجهاد والحج. ويناقش النسفي كلام من قال بغير ذلك، ليبطله، ويدعم رأي أهل السنة والجماعة.
Then if faith were an expression of affirmation (al-tasdiq), declaration (al-iqrar), and action (al-`amal), then the meaning would be that if one of the three pillars is removed, faith is removed, even though Muslims have established its ruling by mere belief before the existence of other actions. Then indeed the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, when Gabriel, peace be upon him, asked him about faith, answered: 'That you believe in Allah, His angels ... etc.' meaning that you affirm. And if faith were other than affirmation, then Gabriel's coming would have confused people, not taught them, and the Prophet would have been deficient in the answer. And the statement of the Prophet when asked about the best deeds: 'Faith, without doubt, and jihad without treachery, and an accepted hajj'—in his speech, peace be upon him, there is a distinction between faith, jihad, and hajj. And al-Nasafi discusses the statement of those who say otherwise, to refute it, and to support the opinion of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama`a.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument and hadith evidence. The logical argument is a reductio: if iman consisted of three components (tasdiq, iqrar, and amal), then removing one would remove iman entirely; but Muslims affirm that iman's ruling is established by mere belief before any actions, which is inconsistent. The hadith evidence: when Jibril asked the Prophet about iman, he answered by listing beliefs (tasdiq), not actions; if iman were broader, Jibril would have misled people. Another hadith lists 'iman' as distinct from 'jihad' and 'hajj', showing iman is separate from actions. The passage concludes that al-Nasafi refutes opposing views to support the Sunni position.

Key Terms
التصديق
الإقرار
العمل
الأركان الثلاثة
أفضل الأعمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman includes actions (amal) as a necessary part, such that without actions iman is incomplete or invalid.
Correction: The logical argument shows that if actions were a pillar, losing an action would lose iman, which is false. Hadith evidence distinguishes iman from actions, proving iman is only tasdiq.

Checkpoint
According to the logical argument, what would happen if iman included affirmation, declaration, and action?
A) It would be stronger
B) Removing any one would remove iman
C) It would be easier to attain
D) It would require all three always

Key Takeaway
The logical and hadith evidence proves that iman is exclusively tasdiq of the heart, not including actions as integral parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your salvation rests on nothing but the affirmation in your heart, or do you secretly rely on your deeds to 'top up' your faith? Take a moment to examine your heart: where is your trust placed?
?
If iman is only tasdiq, then what does that mean for your daily actions? Are they mere fruits of faith or do you treat them as if your faith depends on them? How would your life change if you fully internalized that your iman is complete with only belief?
?
The Prophet distinguished iman from jihad and hajj. Do you sometimes confuse the two? When you fall short in deeds, do you doubt your iman? Commit today to affirming your tasdiq and separating it from your performance.

Nasafi on the Increase and Decrease of Faith
Author's opinion and Nasafi's Q&A on increase/decrease

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view that faith (iman) is essentially heart affirmation (tasdiq) and does not increase or decrease, in contrast to the Ash'ari position.

Core Idea
Faith is a single, unchanging reality—either present or absent.

The modern author opens by affirming that iman is tasdiq, with iqrar as a condition for worldly rulings, citing Abu Hanifa. Then Nasafi directly addresses a contentious question: does faith increase or decrease? His answer firmly grounds iman as a binary quality, not a quantity. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's opening statement
Notice that the modern author gives his personal opinion, aligning with Abu Hanifa, that iman is tasdiq and iqrar is a condition for worldly rulings.
This sets the stage for the Maturidi position.
2
Identify the question Nasafi poses
Nasafi asks: 'Does faith increase and decrease?' This is a key debate between Maturidis and Ash'aris.
3
Understand Nasafi's answer and reasoning
Nasafi answers that faith does not increase by obedience or decrease by sin. He argues that faith is heart affirmation, which is binary—either present (iman) or absent (kufr). There is no gradation.
His reasoning is that tasdiq is a single act, not a quantity.
4
Contrast with the Ash'ari view
The passage mentions that Ash'aris hold that faith increases with good deeds and decreases with others. Reflect on the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أني أحسب أن الإيمان هو التصديق، والإقرار شرط أساسي لإجزاء الأحكام الدنيوية، وهو ما رأيته لأبي حنيفة في الفقه الأبسط، والفقه الأكبر. ويبدأ النسفي بعد ذلك بعرض سؤال: هل يزيد الإيمان وينقص؟ ويجيب: إن الإيمان لا يزيد بالطاعات، ولا ينقص بالمعاصي، لأنه عبارة عن التصديق القلبي وليس فيه نقصان أو زيادة، بل فيه تصديق، وهو الإيمان، وتكذيب، وهو الكفر، وهذا خلافا لقول الأشاعرة حيث يجعلون الإيمان يزيد بالأعمال الصالحة وينقص بغيرها.
Except that I think that faith is affirmation (al-tasdiq), and declaration (al-iqrar) is an essential condition for the validity of worldly rulings, and this is what I have seen from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Absat and al-Fiqh al-Akbar. And al-Nasafi then begins by presenting a question: Does faith increase and decrease? And he answers: Verily, faith does not increase by acts of obedience, nor does it decrease by acts of disobedience, because it is an expression of heart affirmation (al-tasdiq al-qalbi), and there is no decrease or increase in it; rather, there is affirmation—which is faith—and denial (al-takdhib)—which is unbelief (al-kufr). And this is contrary to the statement of the Ash`aris, who make faith increase by good deeds and decrease by other than them.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage begins with the modern author's personal recension: iman is tasdiq, and iqrar is a condition for worldly rulings, citing Abu Hanifa. Then it presents Nasafi's treatment of the question of increase and decrease. Nasafi answers that iman does not increase with obedience or decrease with sin, because it is heart affirmation, which is binary—either present (iman) or absent (kufr). This refutes the Ash'ari view that iman fluctuates with works.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that iman can be more or less depending on deeds.
Correction: Nasafi asserts that iman is not a quantity; it is either present or absent, defined by heart affirmation.

Checkpoint

A) They increase it
B) They decrease it
C) They have no effect on it
D) They are a condition for it

Key Takeaway
Faith (iman) is heart affirmation, a single reality that does not increase or decrease; it is either present or absent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your faith is a single, unchanging reality that cannot increase or decrease, does your emotional experience of closeness to Allah when you obey or distance when you sin reflect a change in your actual iman or just your awareness of it? How can you anchor your identity in the unshakable reality of tasdiq rather than in the fluctuation of your deeds?
?
The passage distinguishes between the essence of faith (tasdiq) and the condition for worldly rulings (iqrar). Are you living as if your verbal declaration and outward compliance are the same as your heart's affirmation? Where have you relied on your tongue or actions to reassure yourself of your iman while neglecting the state of your heart?
?
Knowing that your sins do not decrease your iman, do you feel a dangerous sense of security? Or does it drive you to a deeper gratitude and urgency to ensure your tasdiq is genuine? What specific sin have you justified by saying, 'My iman is still there, so it's okay,' and how can you instead let the binary nature of iman push you to protect it at all costs?

Debating the Final Outcome: Does Earlier Faith Count?
Nasafi's view on the final outcome vs. Khawarij and Ash'ari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's position on the 'aqiba (final outcome) and how it differs from the Khawarij and Ash'ari views regarding the value of earlier faith.

Core Idea
For Nasafi, a moment of true faith is never wasted.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi tackles a delicate theological question: if a person truly believes but later apostasizes and dies as an unbeliever, does their earlier faith have any value? Nasafi argues that it does, rejecting the views of those who say only the final state matters. Let's read his argument and see how it shapes our understanding of faith's enduring worth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Notice the key terms: العاقبة, التصديق, فترة الإيمان.
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify Nasafi's main claim
Find the sentence where Nasafi states his position: 'whoever has affirmation at the moment is a believer.' Understand that he values each moment of faith.
3
Understand the disagreement
The author mentions two opposing views: Najda al-Haruri (Khawarij) and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author says both have no basis. Then he explains that the real disagreement is about a person who believed, then apostatized, and died.
The author adds his own analysis here.
4
Grasp the difference between Nasafi and Ash'ari
For Nasafi, the period of faith is credited. For Ash'ari, only the final state matters. Ask: why does this matter? It shows that faith has lasting value even if later lost.
This connects to the broader Maturidi emphasis on the sincerity of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث عن العاقبة، فيذكر أن من حدث منه التصديق للحال فهو مؤمن، أما من يقول : إن العبرة بالعاقبة كنجدة الحروري من الخوارج، وأبي الحسن الأشعري فكلا لا أصل له. مهم وأرى أن الخلاف بينهما محصور فيمن آمن فترة ثم رجع عن الإيمان، ومات، فعلى كلام النسفي فترة الإيمان محتسبة له ، أما الأشاعرة فلا ينظرون إليها والعبرة عندهم بحالته التي مات عليها.
Then he discusses the final outcome (al-`aqiba), and mentions that whoever has affirmation (al-tasdiq) at the moment is a believer. As for those who say that the consideration is the final outcome—like Najda al-Haruri among the Khawarij, and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash`ari—then both have no basis. Important: And I see that the difference between them is restricted to one who believed for a period then recanted from faith and died; according to Nasafi's statement, the period of faith is credited to him, whereas the Ash`aris do not consider it, and the consideration with them is the state upon which he died.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the issue of 'aqiba (final outcome). Nasafi's position is that if a person has tasdiq at any time, he is a believer at that moment. The author rejects the views of those (like Najda al-Haruri of the Khawarij and al-Ash'ari) who say the only thing that matters is the final state. The author adds his own analysis: the real disagreement is about someone who believed, then apostatized, and died. For Nasafi, the period of faith is counted for him; for the Ash'aris, only his final state matters.

Misconception: Some people think that if a person dies as an unbeliever, their earlier faith is completely worthless and has no effect.
Correction: Nasafi teaches that the period of faith is credited to the person; it is not erased by later apostasy. This is the Maturidi position.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms that a person's period of faith retains its value even if they later apostatize and die in disbelief, unlike the Ash'ari view that only the final state matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Khawarij and Ash'ari both focus on the final outcome, while Nasafi values every moment of faith. Are you someone who judges others (or yourself) by their current state, forgetting past sincerity? What specific step can you take this week to honor the faith of someone who may be struggling, rather than writing them off?
?
Nasafi says the period of faith is credited even for one who dies in disbelief. If a non-Muslim relative once showed signs of belief, does this passage give you a reason to hope for mercy? How should this affect your du‘a and attitude toward them now?

The Certainty of Faith: Rejecting 'Insha'Allah' in Iman
Nasafi explains why iman must be certain and refutes the Ash'ari view on saying 'insha'Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi (Maturidi) holds that saying 'insha'Allah' about one's iman introduces doubt and thus invalidates the certainty required for true faith.

Core Idea
Faith is certainty; 'if Allah wills' implies doubt, negating faith itself.

This lesson delves into a key Maturidi position on the nature of iman. Nasafi argues that faith is not just any affirmation but one that reaches the level of absolute certainty. Therefore, qualifying it with 'insha'Allah' is not a sign of humility but a contradiction that undermines the very essence of faith. We will explore his reasoning and his refutation of the Ash'ari school on this point.

Guided Path
1
Understand Nasafi's definition of iman
Read Nasafi's statement that iman is tasdiq (affirmation) reaching the level of jazm (certainty). Consider why certainty is essential.
Jazm means absolute conviction without any doubt.
2
See why conditionality introduces doubt
Notice that saying 'if Allah wills' introduces a condition (shart), which implies hesitation or doubt. Contrast this with the certainty required.
3
Note the Ash'ari alternative
Read the Ash'ari position as presented: they avoid asserting faith with constancy and say 'insha'Allah'. Consider the theological difference.
This is a point of ikhtilaf between Maturidis and Ash'aris.
4
Analyze the Quranic refutation
Examine the two Quranic verses Nasafi cites: one where Allah testifies that the believers have faith, and another commanding them to say 'we believe'. Reflect on how these verses support absolute certainty.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if iman could be less than certain, would these verses still be decisive? This reinforces the necessity of certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتحدث في مسألة المشيئة فيقول : إن الإيمان هو التصديق البالغ حد الجزم، ولهذا لا يصح أن يقال فيه أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، لأن الشرط هنا يدل على التوقف والشك، وليس على الجزم. فلا يكون إيمانا. أما الأشاعرة فيقولون: لا نقول نحن مؤمنون على الثبات، بل نقول إن شاء الله. ويبطل النسفي كلام الأشاعرة مستشهدا بقوله تعالى: ءَامَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِن رَّبِّهِ وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: ۲۸٥] فقد شهد الله لهم بالإيمان، والقول بغير ذلك باطل، وبقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا ءَامَنَا ﴾ [الحجرات: ١٤] والإيمان تصديق.
And he discusses the matter of the will (al-mashi'a) and says: Verily, faith is affirmation (al-tasdiq) that reaches the level of certainty (al-jazm); for this reason it is not valid to say about it, 'I am a believer if Allah wills,' because the condition here indicates hesitation and doubt, not certainty. So it would not be faith. As for the Ash`aris, they say: We do not say we are believers with constancy, but rather we say 'if Allah wills.' And al-Nasafi refutes the statement of the Ash`aris by citing the statement of the Exalted: 'The Messenger has believed in what was revealed to him from his Lord, and the believers' [al-Baqarah 2:285]; for Allah has testified to their faith, and saying otherwise is false, and by His statement: 'Say, we believe' [al-Hujurat 49:14] and faith is affirmation.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents Nasafi's view on the permissibility of saying 'insha'Allah' about one's iman. He argues that iman is tasdiq that reaches the level of certainty (jazm). Adding 'if Allah wills' introduces conditionality, implying doubt, which contradicts certainty. Thus such a statement would not be valid iman. The Ash'aris hold that one should say 'insha'Allah' instead of asserting faith with certainty. Nasafi refutes them with two Quranic verses: one where Allah testifies that the believers have faith (absolute), and another commanding believers to say 'We believe' (without condition).

Key Terms
المشيئة
الجزم
التصديق
إن شاء الله
الشك
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think saying 'insha'Allah' about faith is humble and shows reliance on Allah.
Correction: Nasafi argues that it introduces doubt, and iman requires certainty. True reliance on Allah does not mean doubting one's own faith.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject saying 'I am a believer if Allah wills'?
A) Because it is disrespectful
B) Because it introduces doubt, and iman requires certainty
C) Because the phrase is only for future actions
D) Because it implies shirk

Key Takeaway
Iman must be affirmed with absolute certainty, and any qualification like 'insha'Allah' introduces doubt, thereby negating the essence of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer,' do you say it with absolute certainty, or is there a hidden hesitation in your heart? What doubt are you holding onto that Nasafi's argument exposes?
?
If you were to stand before Allah right now and be asked about your iman, would your answer be unconditional? What would it take for you to reach that level of certainty?
?
Do you ever use 'insha'Allah' about your faith as a way to avoid arrogance, but in reality are shielding yourself from the full commitment of certainty? What step can you take today to affirm your iman without reservation?

Definition of Iman: Tasdiq and the Role of Iqrar
Nasafi defines iman as tasdiq and clarifies the role of iqrar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's definition of iman as tasdiq and the function of iqrar as a condition for worldly rulings.

Core Idea
Iman is heart affirmation; verbal confession is a condition for worldly application.

Nasafi begins by stating his conviction that iman is essentially tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), while iqrar (verbal confession) serves as a necessary condition for the implementation of Islamic rulings in this world. He attributes this view to Abu Hanifa's works, establishing a foundational Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the two elements
Find tasdiq and iqrar. Understand their roles.
Tasdiq is essence, iqrar is condition.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why iqrar is not part of iman itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أني أحسب أن الإيمان هو التصديق، والإقرار شرط أساسي لإجزاء الأحكام الدنيوية، وهو ما رأيته لأبي حنيفة في الفقه الأبسط، والفقه الأكبر. ويبدأ النسفي بعد ذلك بعرض سؤال: هل يزيد الإيمان وينقص؟ ويجيب: إن الإيمان لا يزيد بالطاعات، ولا ينقص بالمعاصي، لأنه عبارة عن التصديق القلبي وليس فيه نقصان أو زيادة، بل فيه تصديق، وهو الإيمان، وتكذيب، وهو الكفر، وهذا خلافا لقول الأشاعرة حيث يجعلون الإيمان يزيد بالأعمال الصالحة وينقص بغيرها. ويقيم النسفي الأدلة على ما ذهب إليه، ويناقش أدلة الآخرين، ليدعم رأيه بعد إبطال آرائهم. ثم يتحدث عن العاقبة، فيذكر أن من حدث منه التصديق للحال فهو مؤمن، أما من يقول : إن العبرة بالعاقبة كنجدة الحروري من الخوارج، وأبي الحسن الأشعري فكلا لا أصل له. وأرى أن الخلاف بينهما محصور فيمن آمن فترة ثم رجع عن الإيمان، ومات، فعلى كلام النسفي فترة الإيمان محتسبة له ، أما الأشاعرة فلا ينظرون إليها والعبرة عندهم بحالته التي مات عليها. ويتحدث في مسألة المشيئة فيقول : إن الإيمان هو التصديق البالغ حد الجزم، ولهذا لا يصح أن يقال فيه أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، لأن الشرط هنا يدل على التوقف والشك، وليس على الجزم. فلا يكون إيمانا. أما الأشاعرة فيقولون: لا نقول نحن مؤمنون على الثبات، بل نقول إن شاء الله. ويبطل النسفي كلام الأشاعرة مستشهدا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِن رَّبِّهِ وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: 285] فقد شهد الله لهم بالإيمان، والقول بغير ذلك باطل، وبقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا آمَنَّا ﴾ [الحجرات: 14] والإيمان تصديق.
But I think that iman is tasdiq (affirmation), and iqrar (verbal confession) is a basic condition for the implementation of worldly rulings, and that is what I saw from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Absat and al-Fiqh al-Akbar. Then al-Nasafi begins by presenting a question: Does iman increase and decrease? And he answers: Iman does not increase by acts of obedience, nor does it decrease by sins, because it is an expression of tasdiq of the heart, and there is no decrease or increase in it, rather there is tasdiq, which is iman, and takdhib (denial), which is kufr, and this is contrary to the saying of the Ash'aris, who make iman increase by righteous deeds and decrease by other than them. And al-Nasafi establishes evidence for what he holds, and discusses the evidence of others, to support his opinion after invalidating their views. Then he speaks about the final outcome, and mentions that whoever manifests tasdiq at present is a believer; as for those who say that the criterion is the final outcome, like Najda al-Haruri of the Khawarij and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, then neither has any basis. And I see that the disagreement between them is limited to the one who believed for a period then recanted from iman and died: according to al-Nasafi's statement, the period of iman is accounted for him, while the Ash'aris do not consider it and the criterion for them is the state in which he died. And he speaks on the issue of ishta' (volition) and says: Iman is tasdiq that reaches the level of certainty, and therefore it is not valid to say about it 'I am a believer if Allah wills', because the condition here indicates hesitation and doubt, not certainty. So it would not be iman. As for the Ash'aris, they say: We do not say we are believers with firmness, but we say 'if Allah wills'. And al-Nasafi invalidates the Ash'ari statement, citing the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The Messenger believes in what has been revealed to him from his Lord, and the believers' [al-Baqarah 285] — for Allah testified for them with iman, and saying otherwise is false — and by His saying: 'Say: We believe' [al-Hujurat 14] and iman is tasdiq.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a composite of Nasafi's own statement and an editorial report of his views on the nature of iman. It opens with Nasafi stating his personal conviction that iman is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), while iqrar (verbal confession) is a necessary condition for the application of worldly Islamic rulings; he attributes this view to Abu Hanifa's works. The passage then shifts to editorial mode, reporting that al-Nasafi poses the question of whether iman increases and decreases. He answers that iman does not increase by obedience nor decrease by sin, because it is essentially heart-affirmation, which is indivisible (either present or absent). This puts him in opposition to the Ash'aris, who see iman as subject to increase and decrease with deeds. Nasafi rejects the Ash'ari notion that the final outcome ('aqiba) determines one's faith; rather, he holds that tasdiq as soon as it occurs makes one a believer. The editor adds a gloss explaining that the debate relates to someone who believed then apostatized: Nasafi counts the period of belief, while the Ash'aris discount it. Then Nasafi discusses the issue of saying 'in sha' Allah' regarding iman; he holds that such hesitation undermines the certainty (jazzm) required for true iman, so one should not say 'I am a believer if Allah wills'. He supports this with Quranic verses that assert the believers' iman without condition. The Ash'aris allow the phrase to express hope or fear. Nasafi argues that iman is essentially tasdiq, and conditionality contradicts the certainty that tasdiq requires.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
الإقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iqrar is part of the essence of iman.
Correction: Nasafi distinguishes: tasdiq is the essence, iqrar is a condition for worldly rulings.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the essence of iman?
A) Verbal confession
B) Heart affirmation
C) Both equally
D) Actions

Key Takeaway
Iman is fundamentally affirmation of the heart, while verbal declaration is a condition for legal purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your faith is merely verbal - something you say with your tongue but that hasn't truly settled in your heart? What would change in your life if your internal tasdiq matched your external iqrar?
?
If iqrar is only a condition for worldly rulings, and Allah looks at your heart alone, what would He find in your heart right now? Is your iman a deep certainty that shapes your actions, or just a casual statement?
?
The scholars distinguish between the essence of iman and its worldly conditions. Which one have you been prioritizing - the inward reality or outward appearance? Commit to one concrete way today to strengthen your tasdiq.

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
4 min
Does Iman Increase or Decrease?
Nasafi denies increase or decrease of iman and contrasts with Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that iman does not increase or decrease because it is a fixed state of tasdiq.

Core Idea
Iman does not increase or decrease; it is either present or absent.

Nasafi addresses a central question in Islamic theology: can iman increase or decrease? He answers with a clear 'no', arguing that iman is tasdiq of the heart, which is indivisible. This puts him in direct opposition to the Ash'aris, who hold that iman increases with good deeds and decreases with sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Read Nasafi's presentation of the question and his answer.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Understand why tasdiq cannot increase or decrease.
It is a single act of the heart.
3
Compare with Ash'ari view
Contrast Nasafi's view with the Ash'ari position mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أني أحسب أن الإيمان هو التصديق، والإقرار شرط أساسي لإجزاء الأحكام الدنيوية، وهو ما رأيته لأبي حنيفة في الفقه الأبسط، والفقه الأكبر. ويبدأ النسفي بعد ذلك بعرض سؤال: هل يزيد الإيمان وينقص؟ ويجيب: إن الإيمان لا يزيد بالطاعات، ولا ينقص بالمعاصي، لأنه عبارة عن التصديق القلبي وليس فيه نقصان أو زيادة، بل فيه تصديق، وهو الإيمان، وتكذيب، وهو الكفر، وهذا خلافا لقول الأشاعرة حيث يجعلون الإيمان يزيد بالأعمال الصالحة وينقص بغيرها. ويقيم النسفي الأدلة على ما ذهب إليه، ويناقش أدلة الآخرين، ليدعم رأيه بعد إبطال آرائهم. ثم يتحدث عن العاقبة، فيذكر أن من حدث منه التصديق للحال فهو مؤمن، أما من يقول : إن العبرة بالعاقبة كنجدة الحروري من الخوارج، وأبي الحسن الأشعري فكلا لا أصل له. وأرى أن الخلاف بينهما محصور فيمن آمن فترة ثم رجع عن الإيمان، ومات، فعلى كلام النسفي فترة الإيمان محتسبة له ، أما الأشاعرة فلا ينظرون إليها والعبرة عندهم بحالته التي مات عليها. ويتحدث في مسألة المشيئة فيقول : إن الإيمان هو التصديق البالغ حد الجزم، ولهذا لا يصح أن يقال فيه أنا مؤمن إن شاء الله، لأن الشرط هنا يدل على التوقف والشك، وليس على الجزم. فلا يكون إيمانا. أما الأشاعرة فيقولون: لا نقول نحن مؤمنون على الثبات، بل نقول إن شاء الله. ويبطل النسفي كلام الأشاعرة مستشهدا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ آمَنَ الرَّسُولُ بِمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْهِ مِن رَّبِّهِ وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ ﴾ [البقرة: 285] فقد شهد الله لهم بالإيمان، والقول بغير ذلك باطل، وبقوله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا آمَنَّا ﴾ [الحجرات: 14] والإيمان تصديق.
But I think that iman is tasdiq (affirmation), and iqrar (verbal confession) is a basic condition for the implementation of worldly rulings, and that is what I saw from Abu Hanifa in al-Fiqh al-Absat and al-Fiqh al-Akbar. Then al-Nasafi begins by presenting a question: Does iman increase and decrease? And he answers: Iman does not increase by acts of obedience, nor does it decrease by sins, because it is an expression of tasdiq of the heart, and there is no decrease or increase in it, rather there is tasdiq, which is iman, and takdhib (denial), which is kufr, and this is contrary to the saying of the Ash'aris, who make iman increase by righteous deeds and decrease by other than them. And al-Nasafi establishes evidence for what he holds, and discusses the evidence of others, to support his opinion after invalidating their views. Then he speaks about the final outcome, and mentions that whoever manifests tasdiq at present is a believer; as for those who say that the criterion is the final outcome, like Najda al-Haruri of the Khawarij and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, then neither has any basis. And I see that the disagreement between them is limited to the one who believed for a period then recanted from iman and died: according to al-Nasafi's statement, the period of iman is accounted for him, while the Ash'aris do not consider it and the criterion for them is the state in which he died. And he speaks on the issue of ishta' (volition) and says: Iman is tasdiq that reaches the level of certainty, and therefore it is not valid to say about it 'I am a believer if Allah wills', because the condition here indicates hesitation and doubt, not certainty. So it would not be iman. As for the Ash'aris, they say: We do not say we are believers with firmness, but we say 'if Allah wills'. And al-Nasafi invalidates the Ash'ari statement, citing the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The Messenger believes in what has been revealed to him from his Lord, and the believers' [al-Baqarah 285] — for Allah testified for them with iman, and saying otherwise is false — and by His saying: 'Say: We believe' [al-Hujurat 14] and iman is tasdiq.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a composite of Nasafi's own statement and an editorial report of his views on the nature of iman. It opens with Nasafi stating his personal conviction that iman is tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), while iqrar (verbal confession) is a necessary condition for the application of worldly Islamic rulings; he attributes this view to Abu Hanifa's works. The passage then shifts to editorial mode, reporting that al-Nasafi poses the question of whether iman increases and decreases. He answers that iman does not increase by obedience nor decrease by sin, because it is essentially heart-affirmation, which is indivisible (either present or absent). This puts him in opposition to the Ash'aris, who see iman as subject to increase and decrease with deeds. Nasafi rejects the Ash'ari notion that the final outcome ('aqiba) determines one's faith; rather, he holds that tasdiq as soon as it occurs makes one a believer. The editor adds a gloss explaining that the debate relates to someone who believed then apostatized: Nasafi counts the period of belief, while the Ash'aris discount it. Then Nasafi discusses the issue of saying 'in sha' Allah' regarding iman; he holds that such hesitation undermines the certainty (jazzm) required for true iman, so one should not say 'I am a believer if Allah wills'. He supports this with Quranic verses that assert the believers' iman without condition. The Ash'aris allow the phrase to express hope or fear. Nasafi argues that iman is essentially tasdiq, and conditionality contradicts the certainty that tasdiq requires.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الزيادة
النقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Iman can increase with good deeds.
Correction: Nasafi argues iman is fixed, only actions vary.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does iman not increase or decrease?
A) Because it is a confession of the tongue
B) Because it is affirmation of the heart which is not quantifiable
C) Because it depends on God's will
D) Because it is a state of certainty

Key Takeaway
Iman is a fixed internal affirmation, not subject to increase or decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If iman is a fixed state of affirmation and not subject to fluctuation, then when you feel your iman is 'low,' are you actually doubting? Examine your heart: is there any area where you do not fully affirm what Allah has revealed? If you affirm fully, then your iman is complete regardless of your sins. Does that realization bring you peace or unease? Will you now hold onto the certainty of your iman even when your deeds fall short?
?
The Ash'ari view links iman to actions, which means your faith seems to rise and fall with your performance. Nasafi says that is a mistake. Have you ever let your sins make you feel that your iman was weak or deficient? How does it change your self-perception to know that your iman itself never wavers? Will you separate your identity as a believer from your actions, and trust in the steadfastness of your tasdiq?
?
The passage declares that there is only tasdiq (iman) or takdhib (kufr) — no middle ground. Where in your life have you been comfortable with a lukewarm faith, thinking it's okay to have 'some' doubt? Could that be a form of takdhib? Today, will you identify any area where you are not fully affirming and resolve to bring it into tasdiq?

The Criterion: Present State vs. Final Outcome
Nasafi affirms present tasdiq as criterion for faith, rejecting final outcome view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's view that faith is determined by the present state of tasdiq, not by the final outcome at death.

Core Idea
Faith is judged by present affirmation, not by how one dies.

Nasafi addresses another disputed issue: should we consider a person's final outcome (aqiba) or his present state when judging whether he is a believer? He insists that whoever has tasdiq at present is a believer, rejecting the views of those who make the final outcome the criterion, such as Najda al-Haruri and Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The editor adds a nuance regarding those who apostatize.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on aqiba
Read Nasafi's discussion of final outcome.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the contrast between Nasafi and those who focus on final outcome.
3
Understand the apostasy example
Grasp the practical difference explained by the editor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Are you more concerned about how others perceive your final state (e.g., dying as a believer) than about your actual present affirmation? Which truly matters to you now, and what will you do to ensure your present tasdiq is sincere?

Saying 'In sha' Allah' About Iman
Nasafi prohibits conditional statement about iman and cites Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that one should not say 'I am a believer if Allah wills' because iman requires certainty.

Core Idea
Iman requires certainty (jazzm); conditionality contradicts it.

Nasafi tackles the practice of adding 'in sha' Allah' when declaring one's faith. He argues that this introduces doubt and hesitation, which is incompatible with the certainty (jazzm) that iman demands. He supports his position with Quranic verses where Allah testifies to the believers' faith without condition.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument about 'in sha' Allah'
Read Nasafi's critique of the conditional phrase.
2
Review the Quranic evidence
How do the cited verses support Nasafi's point?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am a believer' in your heart, do you ever add a silent 'if Allah wills'? What does that reveal about the certainty of your faith?
?
Allah says 'The believers' (al-Baqarah 285) without any condition. Are you willing to align your own testimony with that verse, or is there a hesitation that holds you back?
?
If your life depended on the certainty of your iman right now, would you be able to declare it unconditionally? What would need to change for you to reach that level of jazzm?

Islam and Iman: One Reality According to Nasafi
Nasafi argues that Islam and iman are synonymous and inseparable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that Islam and iman are synonymous, and how he defends this against the Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Every believer is a Muslim and every Muslim is a believer — they are one and the same.

This passage presents a core Maturidi position: Islam and iman are not two separate things but a single reality. Nasafi argues that no one can be a true believer without being a Muslim, and vice versa. He supports his view with rational and Quranic evidence, and then engages with the opposing Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read Nasafi's claim
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Nasafi flatly states that Islam and iman are one concept.
The passage begins with Nasafi's direct assertion.
2
Identify the definitions
Nasafi defines Islam as total dedication to Allah and iman as affirmation of His sole lordship. See how these definitions converge.
Both definitions point to the same submission.
3
Examine the proofs
Nasafi gives a rational argument (impossible to separate) and two Quranic proofs. Find each and see how they link.
The proofs are: 1) rational inseparability, 2) Quran 3:85, 3) Quran 64:2 and historical classification.
4
Understand the opposing view
The Ash'aris, especially al-Baqillani, cite the Bedouins verse (49:14) and the hadith of Gabriel. See why they think the two are distinct.
The opposing evidence is presented before Nasafi's refutation.
5
Observe the refutation
Nasafi refutes the opposing evidence, arguing that the classification of people in the Prophet's time shows Muslims must be believers.
Refutation is summarized; details are not given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث النسفي عن الإسلام والإيمان فيقول بأن مفهومهما شيء واحد، وأنه لا مغايرة بين الإسلام ،والإيمان، فالإسلام جعل الأشياء كلها لله، والإيمان هو التصديق بأن كل شيء له ومنه سبحانه وهو رب كل شيء، والأسماء من قبيل المترادفات. فكل مسلم مؤمن، وكل مؤمن مسلم. ويقول بعض الأشاعرة ومنهم الباقلاني : إن الإسلام غير الإيمان بدلالة قوله تعالى قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ آمَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا } [الحجرات: 14]، وبحديث الإيمان والإسلام. ويستدل النسفي على قوله: إن الإيمان هو الإسلام، وأنهما شيء واحد بأن أحدهما بدون الأخر يكون محالا ، يوضح ذلك أن من استجمع شرائط الإيمان تستبعد العقول ألا يكون مسلما ، وكذا من أتى بكل شرائط الإسلام ، وكذا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ ﴾ [آل عمران: 85] والإيمان دين، فلو كان غير الإسلام لكان غير مقبول، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَمِنكُمْ كَافِرُ وَمِنكُم مُّؤْمِنٌ [التغابن: 2] فالمسلم في أي قسم إذا لم يكن الإيمان هو الإسلام؟ والناس في عهد الرسول كانوا في أصناف ثلاثة مؤمن، وكافر، ومنافق. فمن أيهم كان المسلم إذا لم يكن مؤمنا؟. ويناقش النسفي أدلة من يقول بأن الإيمان غير الإسلام ليبطلها، ويثبت رأيه.
Then al-Nasafi speaks about Islam and iman, saying that their concepts are one thing, and that there is no difference between Islam and iman. Islam is dedicating all things to Allah, and iman is the affirmation that everything belongs to Him and is from Him, Exalted is He, and He is the Lord of everything, and the names are synonyms. So every Muslim is a believer, and every believer is a Muslim. Some Ash'aris, among them al-Baqillani, say: Islam is other than iman, based on the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'The Bedouins said: We believe. Say: You do not believe, but say: We have submitted' [al-Hujurat 14], and on the hadith of iman and Islam. And al-Nasafi adduces evidence for his statement that iman is Islam and they are one thing: that one without the other is impossible. He clarifies that whoever fulfills the conditions of iman, reason deems it impossible that he not be a Muslim, and likewise whoever fulfills all the conditions of Islam. And also by His saying: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him' [Al 'Imran 85] and iman is a religion, so if it were other than Islam, it would not be accepted. And His saying: 'Some of you are disbelievers and some believers' [al-Taghabun 2] — so in which category is the Muslim if iman is not Islam? And the people in the time of the Messenger were of three types: believer, disbeliever, and hypocrite. So from which of them was the Muslim if he is not a believer? And al-Nasafi discusses the evidence of those who say that iman is other than Islam, in order to invalidate it and establish his view.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is an editorial summary of Nasafi's argument that Islam and iman are identical in meaning. It first reports Nasafi's claim that the two concepts are one: Islam is total dedication to Allah, while iman is the affirmation that everything belongs to Him and comes from Him; the names are synonyms. Consequently, every Muslim is a believer and vice versa. The passage then presents the opposing Ash'ari view (e.g., al-Baqillani) who cite the Quranic verse distinguishing between the Bedouins' claim of iman and being told to say 'aslamna' (we have submitted), as well as the famous hadith of Gabriel that separates Islam, iman, and ihsan as distinct categories. Nasafi counters with three proofs: (1) Reason: It is inconceivable that someone who fulfills the conditions of iman would not be a Muslim, and vice versa. (2) Quran: Allah says any religion other than Islam is rejected, but iman is a religion, so if it were different from Islam it would be rejected—an impossibility given the Quran's praise of believers. (3) The classification of people in the Prophet's time into believers, disbelievers, and hypocrites leaves no separate category for Muslims if they are not believers; therefore, Muslims must be believers. Nasafi then engages in refuting the opposing evidence, seeking to invalidate it and affirm his position.

Key Terms
الإسلام
الإيمان
التصديق
المترادفات
الأشاعرة
الباقلاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think that Islam and iman are two different things, one external and one internal.
Correction: Nasafi argues they are synonyms: the same reality. One cannot exist without the other.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Nasafi's argument that Islam and iman are the same?
A) The hadith of Gabriel clearly distinguishes them.
B) It is rationally impossible for a person to be a believer without being a Muslim.
C) The Bedouins' verse proves they are different.
D) Iman is only submission of the limbs.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi firmly holds that Islam and iman are identical in meaning, so that one cannot exist without the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Islam and iman are truly one, then your outward actions and inward conviction must match perfectly. In which area of your life do you find a gap between what you do and what you believe? What is one step you will take today to align them?
?
Nasafi argues that reason cannot accept a believer who is not a Muslim. Do you live in a way that makes it impossible for others to see you as anything other than a believer? What would need to change?
?
The verse 'Whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will not be accepted from him' is used to equate iman with Islam. Do you treat your daily actions as acts of religion that must be accepted by Allah? How can you ensure your work, relationships, and intentions are all submitted to Him?

Nasafi's Argument for the Identity of Islam and Iman
Nasafi argues for the identity of Islam and Iman with supporting arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's position that Islam and Iman are identical, and the key arguments he uses to support this view.

Core Idea
Islam and Iman are two names for the same reality: total surrender to Allah.

In this passage, al-Nasafi tackles a major theological question: Are Islam and Iman the same or different? He firmly sides with those who say they are identical, and he provides rational, scriptural, and historical evidence. The passage summarizes his key arguments and his response to the opposing view of al-Baqillani. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nasafi uses both rational and scriptural arguments.
2
Identify the claim of identity
Al-Nasafi states that Islam and Iman are one concept, using definitions: Islam as dedication of all things to God, Iman as affirmation that everything is from Him. Recognize that he treats the names as synonyms.
He draws a linguistic and conceptual equivalence.
3
Examine the rational argument
Al-Nasafi argues that it is impossible for someone to have all conditions of Iman without being a Muslim, and vice versa. This shows that the two cannot be separated in reality.
This is the core of his rational proof.
4
Consider the scriptural and historical evidence
He cites Quran 3:85 (religion is Islam, Iman is a religion, so if different it would be rejected) and 64:2 (people are either believers or disbelievers, leaving no third category for Muslims if Iman is not Islam). Also notes the three historical categories: believer, disbeliever, hypocrite.
These verses strengthen his argument from revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث النسفي عن الإسلام والإيمان فيقول بأن مفهومهما شيء واحد، وأنه لا مغايرة بين الإسلام والإيمان، فالإسلام جعل الأشياء كلها لله، والإيمان هو التصديق بأن كل شيء له ومنه سبحانه وهو رب كل شيء، والأسماء من قبيل المترادفات. فكل مسلم مؤمن، وكل مؤمن مسلم. ويقول بعض الأشاعرة ومنهم الباقلاني : إن الإسلام غير الإيمان بدلالة قوله تعالى قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ ءَامَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا } [الحجرات: ١٤]، وبحديث الإيمان والإسلام. ويستدل النسفي على قوله: إن الإيمان هو الإسلام، وأنهما شيء واحد بأن أحدهما بدون الأخر يكون محالا ، يوضح ذلك أن من استجمع شرائط الإيمان تستبعد العقول ألا يكون مسلما ، وكذا من أتى بكل شرائط الإسلام ، وكذا بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَبْتَغِ غَيْرَ الْإِسْلَامِ دِينًا فَلَن يُقْبَلَ مِنْهُ ﴾ [آل عمران: ٨٥] والإيمان دين، فلو كان غير الإسلام لكان غير مقبول، وقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَمَنكُمْ كَافِرُ وَمِنكُم مُّؤْمِنٌ [التغابن: ٢] فالمسلم في أي قسم إذا لم يكن الإيمان هو الإسلام؟ والناس في عهد الرسول كانوا في أصناف ثلاثة مؤمن، وكافر، ومنافق. فمن أيهم كان المسلم إذا لم يكن مؤمنا؟. ويناقش النسفي أدلة من يقول بأن الإيمان غير الإسلام ليبطلها، ويثبت رأيه.
Then al-Nasafi discusses Islam and Iman, saying that their concepts are one thing, and that there is no differentiation between Islam and Iman. Islam is the dedication of all things to God, and Iman is the affirmation that everything is from Him and belongs to Him and He is the Lord of everything; the names are of the type of synonyms. So every Muslim is a believer and every believer is a Muslim. Some Ash'aris, among them al-Baqillani, say that Islam is other than Iman, as indicated by His saying : 'The Bedouins said, “We believe.” Say, “You have not believed, but rather say, 'We have submitted.'” [al-Hujurat: 14] and by the hadith of Iman and Islam. Al-Nasafi argues for his statement that Iman is Islam and that they are one thing by saying that one without the other is impossible. He clarifies that for one who fulfills all the conditions of Iman, it is inconceivable that he would not be a Muslim, and likewise for one who brings all the conditions of Islam. Also by His saying: 'And whoever seeks a religion other than Islam, it will never be accepted from him' [Aal-e-Imran: 85] and Iman is a religion, so if it were other than Islam it would not be accepted. And His saying: 'Some of you are disbelievers and some of you are believers' [al-Taghabun: 2]. So in which category is the Muslim if Iman is not Islam? And the people at the time of the Messenger were of three types: believer, disbeliever, and hypocrite. In which of them was the Muslim if he was not a believer? And al-Nasafi discusses the proofs of those who say Iman is other than Islam in order to refute them and establish his view.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a modern summary of al-Nasafi's position on the relationship between Islam and Iman. The author begins by stating that al-Nasafi considers Islam and Iman to be identical in meaning—both denote surrender to God and affirmation that everything is from Him. He argues that the terms are synonymous, so every Muslim is a believer and vice versa. The passage then presents the opposing view of some Ash'aris, notably al-Baqillani, who cite Quran 49:14 and a hadith to differentiate between Islam and Iman. Al-Nasafi counters with rational arguments: one cannot possess all conditions of Iman without being a Muslim, and vice versa. He also uses Quranic verses: 3:85 (religion is Islam, so if Iman were different it would be rejected) and 64:2 (people are divided into believers and disbelievers; if a Muslim is not a believer, which category does he fall into?). The historical reality of only three categories (believer, disbeliever, hypocrite) further supports the identity. The passage concludes that al-Nasafi systematically refutes the opposing proofs and upholds his view.

Checkpoint

A) They are both conditions for salvation.
B) One cannot be a Muslim without being a believer, and vice versa.
C) The Quran uses them interchangeably throughout.
D) The Ash'ari position is logically incoherent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi insists that Islam and Iman are inseparable realities, and any apparent distinction in language does not imply a difference in essence.

?
