Refutation of Ash'ari's Intermediate State
Maturidi refutation of Ash'ari's intermediate state between faith and disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi tradition rejects the Ash'ari position that a person with tasdiq but no istidlal occupies an intermediate state between faith and disbelief.

Core Idea
Ash'ari's intermediate position is internally contradictory and leads to absurd consequences.

This passage presents the Maturidi critique of Ash'ari's claim that a person who assents (tasdiq) but does not engage in rational inference (istidlal) is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. The author argues that this view is linguistically and logically flawed, and that it inadvertently adopts the Mu'tazilite doctrine of 'al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn' which Ash'ari himself rejects. By analyzing Ash'ari's own statements, the Maturidi shows that his position is self-contradictory and leads to impossible conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic argument
Read how the author uses the transitive nature of 'tasdiq' versus 'iman' to question Ash'ari's distinction. Note that Arabic verbs can take prepositions without changing meaning.
2
Identify the logical contradiction
Focus on the claim that Ash'ari removes the person from kufr but does not enter him into iman. Ask: if tasdiq repels kufr, why isn't it iman?
3
Compare with Mu'tazila and fasiq
See how the author draws a parallel between Ash'ari's position and the Mu'tazilite 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn', and then examines Ash'ari's treatment of the person as a fasiq.
4
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Read the final part where the author shows that if tasdiq is not iman, it becomes takdhib, which is absurd. This demonstrates the impossibility of Ash'ari's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبارة عن التصديق فإنه المتعلق بالمخبر، إلا أنه يتعدى إلى المفعول بدون حرف العلة ولفظ الإيمان يتعدى إلى المفعول بالصلة، وكم من فعل تعديه العرب بالصلات، وإن كان يجوز في الأصل التعدية بدون الحرف، يقال: شكرت لفلان ونصحت له، فإذا كان الأمر كذلك بطل هذا الكلام، والذي يدل على بطلانه على أصل الأشعري أن عنده يخرج به عن الكفر واسطة بين الكفر والإيمان ولو لم يكن الإيمان موجودا لما اندفع به الكفر، ثم هذا من الأشعري مناقضة ظاهرة، حيث أخرجه بهذا من الكفر وما أدخله به في الإيمان، وهذا منه إثبات المنزلة بين المنزلتين، كما فعلت المعتزلة وهو يأبى ذلك، ثم العجب منه أن يجعله بمنزلة فساق أهل الملة حيث قال: يعذب بقدر ذنبه وهو ترك الاستدلال - ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة، ولو عفى عنه فأدخله الجنة ابتداء جاز، وهذا هو حكم الفساق، فإن كان فاسقا لتركه النظر، لماذا لم يحكم بإسلامه والكبيرة عندنا لا تنافي الإسلام؟ وإن يكن فعله إيمانا لماذا لم يبق على الكفر، وهو لا ينفي ألا يوجد الإيمان، فإثبات المنزلة بين المنزلتين من أين؟ ثم كما أن المعتزلة اخطأوا حيث أثبتوا الخلود في النيران لمن ليس بكافر فكذا الأشعري أخطأ حيث أدخل الجنة من ليس بمؤمن، يحققه أن الثواب يعرف بوعد الله تعالى والله تعالى ما وعد الجنة إلا للمؤمنين فبأي دليل يقول بدخول من ليس بمؤمن في الجنة؟ ولو قال: أنا لا أقول بأنه مؤمن وأنه كافر وهو يخلد في النار فيقال له: كيف يكون كافرا وقد تبدل منه التكذيب بالتصديق وأنت تقول: إن الإيمان هو التصديق، والكفر هو التكذيب؟ فإن لم يجعل تصديقه إيمانا لأنه لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان فلماذا جعلت تصديقه الثابت في الحقيقة تكذيبا؟ وهل هذا إلا كجعل السكون حركة، والسواد بياضا؟ وكل ذلك مستحيل متناقض والله الموفق.
It is an expression of tasdiq (assent), for it is related to the informant, except that it is transitive to the object without a particle of cause, while the word iman is transitive to the object with a particle. And many verbs are made transitive by the Arabs with particles, even though originally it is permissible to make them transitive without the particle. It is said: 'I thanked so-and-so' (shakartu li-fulan) and 'I advised him' (nasaltu lahu). So when this is the case, this statement is invalid. And what indicates its invalidity according to Ash'ari's principle is that with him (Ash'ari) a person departs by it (tasdiq) from kufr, but there is an intermediary between kufr and iman. If iman did not exist, kufr would not be repelled by it. Then this from Ash'ari is an apparent contradiction: he removed him by this from kufr but did not enter him thereby into iman. This is from him an affirmation of the station between the two stations (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn), as the Mu'tazila did, while he rejects that. Then amazing from him is that he makes him like the sinful (fussaq) of the people of the religion, where he said: 'He is punished according to his sin, which is abandoning istidlal (inference), then his final outcome is paradise. And if He forgives him and enters him into paradise initially, it is permissible.' And this is the ruling for the sinful (fussaq). If he is a sinner (fasiq) for abandoning nazar (consideration), why is he not judged to be a Muslim, while the major sin (kabira) with us does not contradict Islam? And if his act is iman, why does he not remain upon kufr? And he does not negate that iman exists? So from where is the affirmation of the station between the two stations? Then, just as the Mu'tazila erred when they affirmed eternal stay in the Fire for those who are not disbelievers, likewise Ash'ari erred when he entered into paradise one who is not a believer. This is verified by the fact that reward is known by the promise of Allah Most High, and Allah Most High did not promise paradise except to the believers. So by what evidence does he say that a non-believer enters paradise? And if he says: 'I do not say that he is a believer nor that he is a disbeliever, and he will be eternally in the Fire,' it is said to him: 'How can he be a disbeliever while he has replaced takdhib (disbelief) with tasdiq, and you say that iman is tasdiq and kufr is takdhib?' If he does not make his tasdiq iman because he did not enter himself into safety (aman), then why did you make his tasdiq, established in reality, into takdhib? Is this not like making stillness a movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari position on faith. The author begins by noting that the term 'iman' is linguistically used with a preposition (bi) while 'tasdiq' is transitive without a preposition, but Arabic verbs often take prepositions. He then states that Ash'ari's claim that a person who has tasdiq but does not engage in istidlal is neither a believer nor a disbeliever but in an intermediate station is contradictory. He argues that Ash'ari's own principle should make such a person a believer because the tasdiq removes him from kufr. If he is not a believer, then kufr would not be repelled, but Ash'ari says he is not a kafir. This is a contradiction. The author accuses Ash'ari of adopting the Mu'tazilite doctrine of manzila bayn al-manzilatayn, which Ash'ari rejects. Moreover, Ash'ari treats such a person like a fasiq (grave sinner) from the Muslim community, punishing him for his sin (abandoning istidlal) but eventually admitting him to paradise. The author questions why, if he is a fasiq for abandoning nazar, he is not considered a Muslim (since major sin does not negate Islam in Maturidi view). And if his act (tasdiq) is iman, why does he remain in kufr? The author concludes that the Ash'ari position is self-contradictory. He further argues that God only promised paradise to believers, so how can Ash'ari promise it to a non-believer? If Ash'ari says the person is a kafir, then how can his tasdiq not be considered takdhib (disbelief)? The author likens this to claiming stillness is motion or blackness is whiteness, which is absurd.

Key Terms
تصديق
إيمان
كفر
منزلة بين المنزلتين
فساق
استدلال
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'ari's position that a person with tasdiq but no istidlal is in an intermediate state between faith and disbelief is a coherent third option.
Correction: The Maturidi author demonstrates that this position is logically contradictory: if tasdiq repels kufr, it must be iman; otherwise, repelling kufr is impossible. The intermediate state collapses into absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does the author accuse Ash'ari of adopting by positing an intermediate state between iman and kufr?
A) The position of the Mu'tazila on al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn
B) The position of the Khawarij on takfir
C) The position of the Jahmiyya on irja'
D) The position of the Shi'a on imama

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's intermediate state between iman and kufr is impossible because tasdiq either is iman (repelling kufr) or is not; both options lead to contradictions with Ash'ari's own definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author shows that holding two contradictory positions at once leads to absurdity. In your own life, are there areas where you try to maintain a comfortable middle ground between clear obligations and your own desires? What inconsistency are you unwilling to confront?
?
This lesson argues that tasdiq either makes you a believer or it does not—there is no safe third option. Do you live with the full commitment of faith, or do you hold back, hoping that a little assent will keep you safe without requiring full submission? What would change if you acted on your tasdiq as complete iman?
?
Allah only promised paradise to believers. Are you certain that you are among them, or do you secretly rely on a 'middle state' that this passage proves impossible? What practical step can you take today to move from mere assent to true iman that enters you into safety?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite View on the Major Sinner and the Person with Doubts
Refutation of Mu'tazila's view on status of fasiq and shubha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi author argues that the Mu'tazilite position on the committer of a major sin and the person facing doubts is internally inconsistent.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites' own principles contradict their conclusions about faith and sin.

In this passage, the Maturidi theologian critiques the Mu'tazilite stance on two groups: the committer of a major sin (fasiq) and the person grappling with a doubt (shubha). He shows that their classification leads to contradictions. Let's read the argument and see why it fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's strong language: 'bāṭil aydan' (also invalid).
2
Identify the two cases discussed
The passage addresses two types of people: the committer of a major sin (sāḥib al-kabīra) and the one who faces a doubt (man tawajjahat ʿalayhi shubha). Find where each is mentioned.
The author treats them separately, pointing out problems with the Mu'tazilite treatment of each.
3
Analyze the contradiction for the major sinner
The author says the major sinner has tasdiq that brings him into safety (amān), fulfilling conditions of iman, yet Mu'tazila deny him the title of believer. Identify why this is contradictory.
If tasdiq is present and conditions met, consistency would require calling him a believer.
4
Analyze the case of the person with doubt
For the person seeking to repel a doubt, the author says there is no guarantee of success. Until success, the truth of his creed is not apparent. But if his belief remains undisturbed, he remains a believer. See how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.
The Mu'tazila likely considered such a person as not fully believing, but the author insists on preserving iman as long as conviction is steady.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author ends by declaring the Mu'tazilite school inconsistent and self-contradictory. Consider how this critique fits within the larger Maturidi project of defending orthodox faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الكلام على أصول المعتزلة باطل أيضا، لأن صاحب الكبيرة وجد منه التصديق على وجه أدخل نفسه في الأمان، وتحقق بحده وشرطه وهو مع هذا ليس بمؤمن ومن توجهت عليه شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها بعد وهو في طلب وجه الدفع، فلا أمان له من انعدام الظفر بما يتمكن به من دفعها وما لم يتمكن من ذلك لا يظهر أن ما ذهب إليه من العقيدة صحيح إذ الحق ما غلبت حُجَجه وأظهر أسباب التمويه في غيره، وإذا لم يظهر ذلك لم يعرف أنه لم يكن مكذوبا فيما أخبر بصحة هذا إذا كان في طلب دفعها ولم يضطرب في اعتقاده بقي مؤمنا، دل ومع أن هذا الكلام فاسد ولا استمرار لمذهبهم بل هي مذاهب متناقضة، ينقض البعض البعض، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لدينه القويم وصراطه المستقيم.
This statement is also invalid according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has manifested tasdiq in a way that brings himself into safety (aman), and it is realized by its definition and condition, yet with all this he is not a believer. And one who is faced with a doubt (shubha) and is not yet able to repel it while he is seeking a way to repel it, then there is no safety for him from failure to attain what he can repel it with. And as long as he does not attain that, it is not apparent that what he holds of creed is correct, for the truth is that whose arguments prevail and the means of obfuscation in others are made apparent. And if that does not become apparent, it is not known that he is not lying in what he reports as truth. This is if he is seeking to repel the doubt and does not become disturbed in his belief; he remains a believer. Therefore, this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some refuting others. And praise be to Allah who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage critiques the Mu'tazilite position on the major sinner (fasiq) and the person facing doubts (shubha). The author argues that according to Mu'tazilite principles, a person who commits a major sin has tasdiq that brings him into 'aman' (safety) and fulfills the conditions of iman, yet they do not consider him a believer. This is inconsistent. Regarding the person facing a doubt, the author says that if he is actively seeking to repel the doubt, there is no guarantee he will succeed. Until he succeeds, it cannot be known that his belief is correct, because truth is known by its arguments prevailing. However, if his belief remains firm without disturbance, he remains a believer. The author concludes that the Mu'tazilite position is self-contradictory and inconsistent across their various schools. He ends with gratitude to Allah for guidance.

Correction: The Maturidi author argues that if the person has tasdiq that brings them into safety (aman), they should be considered a believer; the Mu'tazilite position is inconsistent.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position on the major sinner and the doubter is self-contradictory and does not hold up to scrutiny.

The author condemns the inconsistency of the Mu'tazila: they held contradictory positions. ?

The Purpose of Tasdiq: Establishing the Rulings of Islam
Answer to the objection that tasdiq must be preceded by 'ilm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi response to the objection about tasdiq being based on knowledge focuses on the practical rulings (ahkam) of Islam rather than the mere label of iman.

Core Idea
The goal of faith is to secure the rulings of Islam, not just the name 'iman'.

In this passage, the author addresses the objection that true iman must be preceded by knowledge. He counters that even without knowledge, the simple act of tasdiq is sufficient to remove a person from kufr and entitle them to the promised reward. The Maturidi perspective shifts the focus from the label to the practical consequences: what matters is that the person is treated as a believer in this world and the next, not whether they can be called 'mu'min' in the fullest sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing too deeply. Then read the literal translation to get the gist. Note that the author is responding to an objection, so he begins with 'قلنا' (we say).
2
Identify the two parts of the response
The response has two main moves: first, an assertion that even without knowledge, tasdiq removes kufr and earns reward; second, a logical turn where the author says the opponent's own concession for establishing rulings can be used to justify the name.
3
Understand the key distinction
Notice the distinction between 'ahkam' (rulings) and 'ism' (name). The author argues that the name is pointless without the rulings, and the loss of the name is harmless if the rulings remain. This is central to the Maturidi position.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this argument is important for Maturidi theology. It shows that iman is defined by its effect (tasdiq) rather than by the means (knowledge). This allows a more inclusive understanding of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن التصديق ينبغي أن يكون عن علم ، قلنا: ومع هذا عند الأشعري بهذا التصديق، يخرج عن الكفر ويستحق الثواب الموعود للمؤمن، ونحن لا نروم إلا هذا، إذ غرضنا من هذا إثبات أحكام الإسلام، لا إطلاق العبارة إذ لا ينفع بإطلاق التسمية مع انعدام الأحكام، ولا ضرر بزوال التسمية مع بقاء أحكام الإسلام، ثم نقول له : كل عذر لك في إثبات الأحكام فهو عذرنا في إطلاق الاسم، يحققه أن الاستدلال وتحصيل العلم ليتوصل بهما إلى التصديق الذي هو المقصد
And their saying: 'Verily, tasdiq must be based on knowledge' – we say: Even so, according to Ash'ari, by this tasdiq, he leaves kufr and deserves the promised reward for the believer. We desire nothing but this, for our purpose in this is to establish the rulings (ahkam) of Islam, not to use the expression, for there is no benefit in applying the name while the rulings are absent, and there is no harm in the removal of the name while the rulings of Islam remain. Then we say to him: Every excuse you have for establishing the rulings is our excuse for applying the name. This is verified by the fact that istidlal (inference) and acquisition of knowledge are only to reach the tasdiq that is the intended goal.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection: that tasdiq must be based on 'ilm (knowledge) to count as iman. The author counters that even according to Ash'ari, this very tasdiq (even without 'ilm?) removes the person from kufr and qualifies him for the reward promised to believers. The author states that his aim is only to establish the rulings (ahkam) of Islam for such a person, not to insist on the label 'iman'. He argues that it is useless to have the name without the rulings, and no harm in losing the name while the rulings remain. Then he turns the argument: any excuse (concession) the opponent offers for establishing the rulings can be used by the Maturidi to apply the name. The reality is that istidlal and acquisition of knowledge are only means to reach the tasdiq, which is the ultimate goal. So if tasdiq is present, the goal is achieved, regardless of whether the means were employed.

Key Terms

تصديق
أحكام الإسلام
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq must be preceded by knowledge to be considered iman and have any effect.
Correction: Tasdiq alone, even without prior knowledge, is sufficient to remove kufr, establish the ahkam of Islam, and entitle one to reward.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the benefit of the name 'iman' when its rulings are absent?
A) The name is essential for salvation
B) There is no benefit in applying the name when the rulings are absent
C) The name is irrelevant even if rulings are present
D) The name is more important than the rulings

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq, even without prior knowledge, is sufficient to remove a person from kufr and entitle them to the rulings of Islam in this world and the reward in the next.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a believer, do you feel a deeper sense of responsibility to live by the rulings of Islam, or do you sometimes settle for the label without the substance? Which matters more to you right now?
?
Have you ever judged someone else’s faith based on their level of knowledge or sophistication, instead of recognizing that simple tasdiq can be enough? How might that affect your treatment of others?
?
If the name 'iman' were taken away from you but all the rulings of Islam still applied, would you be content? Or is your attachment to the label greater than your attachment to the practical obligations?

The Absurdity of Denying Tasdiq without Aman
Initial reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that equating tasdiq without aman to kufr is a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Calling tasdiq without aman kufr is like calling stillness movement.

The author begins by exposing the absurdity of the Mu'tazila claim. He argues that if a person's tasdiq (assent) is genuine, it cannot be considered disbelief simply because it does not bring about 'aman' (security) in the Mu'tazila sense. This leads to a logical contradiction, as he demonstrates with a striking analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the rhetorical question and the analogy.
The author uses a strong analogy to highlight the absurdity.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
The passage presupposes the Mu'tazila claim: tasdiq without aman is not faith but kufr. Look for how the author refutes this.
3
Analyze the analogy
The author compares the claim to 'making stillness movement'. Reflect on why this analogy is powerful.
Both are opposites; equating them is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that this position is 'impossible and contradictory'. This sets the stage for further refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصديقه إيمانا لأنه لم يدخل نفسه في الأمان فلماذا جعلت تصديقه الثابت في الحقيقة تكذيبا؟ وهل هذا إلا كجعل السكون حركة، والسواد بياضا؟ وكل ذلك مستحيل متناقض والله الموفق. وهذا الكلام على أصول المعتزلة باطل أيضا، لأن صاحب الكبيرة وجد منه التصديق على وجه أدخل نفسه في الأمان، وتحقق بحده وشرطه وهو مع هذا ليس بمؤمن ومن توجهت عليه شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها بعد وهو في طلب وجه الدفع، فلا أمان له من انعدام الظفر بما يتمكن به من دفعها وما لم يتمكن من ذلك لا يظهر أن ما ذهب إليه من العقيدة صحيح إذ الحق ما غلبت حُجَجه وأظهر أسباب التمويه في غيره، وإذا لم يظهر ذلك لم يعرف أنه لم يكن مكذوبا فيما أخبر بصحة هذا إذا كان في طلب دفعها ولم يضطرب في اعتقاده بقي مؤمنا، دل ومع أن هذا الكلام فاسد ولا استمرار لمذهبهم بل هي مذاهب متناقضة، ينقض البعض البعض، والحمد لله الذي هدانا لدينه القويم وصراطه المستقيم.
His tasdiq (assent) is faith because he did not enter himself into security (aman). So why do you make his tasdiq, which is established in reality, tantamount to disbelief? Is this not like making stillness movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory, and God is the Grantor of success. This statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq from him in a manner that entered him into security, and is realized by its definition and condition, yet he is not a believer according to them. Also, one who is confronted by an ambiguity (shubha) which he cannot yet repel, while he is seeking a means of repelling it, has no security from failure to attain that by which he can repel it. And if he cannot do that, it does not become apparent that what he holds of belief is correct, for the truth is that whose evidences overcome and whose [opponent's] means of falsification become manifest. If that does not become apparent, it is not known that he was not lying regarding what he affirmed of the soundness of this [belief], while he is seeking to repel it and is not perturbed in his conviction, he remains a believer. [The text says: 'dalla wa ma'a anna hadha al-kalam fasid'] And this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some contradicting others. Praise be to God who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazila argument about the relationship between tasdiq (assent) and aman (security) in defining faith. The author first presents the Mu'tazila claim: if someone's tasdiq does not bring them into a state of aman, then it is not true faith but rather kufr. The author rejects this as impossible, comparing it to turning stillness into movement, a logical contradiction. He then argues that even on Mu'tazila principles this claim is false. He gives two examples: first, the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq that does bring him into aman according to their own definition, yet they do not call him a believer—a contradiction. Second, a person faced with an ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet repel, while sincerely seeking to refute it, remains a believer because his inability does not negate his conviction. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila position is internally inconsistent and their different opinions contradict each other. He ends with praise to God.

Key Terms
تصديق
أمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasdiq without aman is not true faith but kufr.
Correction: The author shows this is logically impossible, as it confuses two opposite categories.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to refute the Mu'tazila claim?
A) Making stillness movement and blackness whiteness.
B) Making fire water and water fire.
C) Making day night and night day.
D) Making life death and death life.

Key Takeaway
Equating tasdiq without aman to kufr is a logical absurdity akin to calling opposites identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever labeled something as 'bad' or 'invalid' simply because it didn't meet your own criteria, even when it was clearly good? The Mu'tazila called tasdiq without aman 'kufr'—an absurd reversal. Where in your life have you confused a thing with its opposite? What would it take to see clearly and ask Allah to guide you to the truth?
?
The author ends with 'wa Allah al-muwaffiq'—acknowledging that only God grants success in recognizing truth. Do you truly rely on Allah to show you the reality of things, or do you trust your own reasoning to distinguish right from wrong? When have you insisted on a position that, if pressed, leads to a contradiction? Will you humble yourself before the truth today?
?
The analogy of stillness and movement shows that calling a thing its opposite is impossible. Is there an area of your faith where you are trying to combine two opposites—like claiming to believe in Allah while living as if He does not see you? What 'stillness' have you called 'movement' in your own actions? Repent and seek His guidance to align your heart and deeds.

The Contradiction of the Committer of Major Sin
First internal contradiction: sahib al-kabira has tasdiq with aman but is not a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how the author exposes a contradiction in the Mu'tazila position by pointing out that the committer of a major sin fulfills their conditions for tasdiq bringing aman, yet is not considered a believer.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila contradict themselves: the sinner has tasdiq with aman but is not a believer.

After the initial reductio, the author attacks the Mu'tazila on their own terms. He presents the case of the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira), who according to Mu'tazila principles has tasdiq that does bring him into aman, yet they still deny him the status of believer. This reveals an internal inconsistency in their doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the author says 'this statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila'.
He is using their own principles against them.
2
Identify the example
The example is the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira). According to Mu'tazila, his tasdiq does bring aman because it meets the definition and condition.
3
Spot the contradiction
Despite having tasdiq with aman, the Mu'tazila do not call this person a believer. This contradicts their claim that tasdiq without aman is kufr, because here tasdiq with aman is still not considered faith.
Thus, their position is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His tasdiq (assent) is faith because he did not enter himself into security (aman). So why do you make his tasdiq, which is established in reality, tantamount to disbelief? Is this not like making stillness movement, and blackness whiteness? All of that is impossible and contradictory, and God is the Grantor of success. This statement is also false according to the principles of the Mu'tazila, because the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq from him in a manner that entered him into security, and is realized by its definition and condition, yet he is not a believer according to them. Also, one who is confronted by an ambiguity (shubha) which he cannot yet repel, while he is seeking a means of repelling it, has no security from failure to attain that by which he can repel it. And if he cannot do that, it does not become apparent that what he holds of belief is correct, for the truth is that whose evidences overcome and whose [opponent's] means of falsification become manifest. If that does not become apparent, it is not known that he was not lying regarding what he affirmed of the soundness of this [belief], while he is seeking to repel it and is not perturbed in his conviction, he remains a believer. [The text says: 'dalla wa ma'a anna hadha al-kalam fasid'] And this statement is corrupt and there is no consistency to their school; rather they are contradictory schools, some contradicting others. Praise be to God who guided us to His upright religion and straight path.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazila argument about the relationship between tasdiq (assent) and aman (security) in defining faith. The author first presents the Mu'tazila claim: if someone's tasdiq does not bring them into a state of aman, then it is not true faith but rather kufr. The author rejects this as impossible, comparing it to turning stillness into movement, a logical contradiction. He then argues that even on Mu'tazila principles this claim is false. He gives two examples: first, the committer of a major sin (sahib al-kabira) has tasdiq that does bring him into aman according to their own definition, yet they do not call him a believer—a contradiction. Second, a person faced with an ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet repel, while sincerely seeking to refute it, remains a believer because his inability does not negate his conviction. The author concludes that the Mu'tazila position is internally inconsistent and their different opinions contradict each other. He ends with praise to God.

Key Terms
كبيرة
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to show the Mu'tazila contradict their own definition?
A) The committer of a major sin has tasdiq that brings aman yet is not considered a believer.
B) A Muslim who never commits sins always has aman.
C) The Mu'tazila require public declaration of faith.
D) The author shows that only prophets can have tasdiq with aman.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim collapses because their own example of the major sinner contradicts their definition of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does the Mu'tazila's inconsistency challenge you to examine your own understanding of faith—do you sometimes define faith in a way that excludes actions, yet still consider yourself a believer? What would it take for you to align your definition with your actual beliefs?
?
When you commit a sin, do you ever doubt your own faith? The Mu'tazila made a logical error by separating tasdiq from belief. How does this expose the danger of defining faith by human logic rather than revelation? In what areas of your life are you tempted to rely on your own reasoning over divine guidance, and what step can you take today to submit more fully?

The Case of the Ambiguity-Seeker
Second internal contradiction: the shubha-seeker lacks aman but remains a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a person confronted with an unresolved ambiguity (shubha) but seeking to repel it remains a believer according to the author.

Core Idea
A believer facing an unresolved shubha remains a believer if he is seeking the truth.

The author continues his refutation by addressing another scenario: a person who encounters a theological ambiguity (shubha) that he cannot yet resolve. The Mu'tazila might argue that such a person lacks aman and therefore is not a believer. The author argues that as long as the person is sincerely seeking to repel the shubha and has not abandoned his conviction, he remains a believer. This further undermines the Mu'tazila equation of aman with faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a longer, more involved argument. Pay attention to the condition and conclusion.
2
Understand the scenario
A person faces a shubha (ambiguity) that he cannot yet repel. He is actively seeking means to repel it. According to the Mu'tazila, he has no aman.
3
Follow the logic
The author argues that inability to repel does not show that his belief is false. Truth is determined by overwhelming evidence, not by the temporary inability to refute a doubt.
4
Identify the conclusion
As long as his heart is not perturbed and he is seeking the truth, he remains a believer. Thus, aman is not a condition for faith.
5
See the final critique
The author ends by declaring the Mu'tazila positions mutually contradictory and praises God for guidance.

Tasdiq Without Knowledge: Sufficiency for Faith
Defends sufficiency of tasdiq without knowledge against Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why tasdiq alone suffices to exit disbelief and establish the rulings of Islam, even if not preceded by knowledge, according to the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
Tasdiq itself is the goal; knowledge is only a means, and the means drop when the end is achieved.

In this passage, the author addresses the Mu'tazila contention that valid tasdiq must be based on knowledge. He argues that even if knowledge is lacking, the tasdiq itself removes one from kufr and entitles one to the Islamic rulings. The author does not see the name 'believer' as crucial; what matters are the legal effects. He then presents a parallel argument: just as the Mu'tazila accept that one in a state of seeking knowledge is a believer, so too can tasdiq itself be sufficient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening sentence: the Mu'tazila require that tasdiq be based on knowledge. Understand their position.
The author states their claim concisely.
2
Examine the author's response
Notice that the author concedes the point for argument but shifts the focus to the rulings (ahkam) rather than the name. He emphasizes that the goal is to establish Islamic rulings, not to argue about nomenclature.
Key phrase: 'We aim for nothing but this.'
3
Understand the analogy
The author uses two analogies: (1) inference and knowledge are means to tasdiq, and once the objective is reached, the means are unnecessary. (2) The Mu'tazila themselves consider a seeker who has not yet attained knowledge as a believer due to his state. The same principle applies here.
Follow the reasoning: means vs. end, and the countervailing condition (mu'arada).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: إن التصديق ينبغي أن يكون عن علم، قلنا: ومع هذا عند الأشعري بهذا التصديق، يخرج عن الكفر ويستحق الثواب الموعود للمؤمن، ونحن لا نروم إلا هذا، إذ غرضنا من هذا إثبات أحكام الإسلام، لا إطلاق العبارة إذ لا ينفع بإطلاق التسمية مع انعدام الأحكام، ولا ضرر بزوال التسمية مع بقاء أحكام الإسلام، ثم نقول له: كل عذر لك في إثبات الأحكام فهو عذرنا في إطلاق الاسم، يحققه أن الاستدلال وتحصيل العلم ليتوصل بهما إلى التصديق الذي هو المقصد المأمور به، فإذ وصل إلى المقصود به وأتى بما أمر على وجهه كان معتبرا، ولا عبرة لانعدام ما ثبت ذريعة له عند حصول المقصود بدونه وكذا بهذا عند المعتزلة يخرج عن الكفر، يحققه أن حالة طلب ما يتمكن به من دفع الشبهة كان مؤمنا وإن انعدم العلم لثبوت المعارضة للحال لما ذكرنا وكذا هذا، وكل ما يعتمدون عليه هناك فهو معتمدنا هاهنا.
And what they said: 'Tasdiq should be based on knowledge.' We say: Even so, according to al-Ash'ari, by this tasdiq one exits from kufr and deserves the promised reward for the believer. We aim for nothing but this, for our purpose in this is to establish the rulings of Islam, not to pronounce the expression [of faith], since the pronouncement of the name is of no benefit when the rulings are absent, and there is no harm in the removal of the name while the rulings of Islam remain. Then we say to him: Every excuse you have in establishing the rulings is our excuse in applying the name. This is substantiated by the fact that inference (istidlal) and acquisition of knowledge are employed to reach the tasdiq which is the commanded objective. So when one attains the objective by it and performs what is commanded in its proper manner, it is considered valid, and no attention is paid to the absence of what was established as a means to it when the objective is achieved without it. Similarly, by this, according to the Mu'tazila, one exits from kufr. This is substantiated by the fact that the one in a state of seeking what enables him to repel the ambiguity is a believer even if knowledge is absent, due to the existence of a countervailing condition (al-mu'arada li'l-hal) as we mentioned. And this is similar. And everything they rely on there is our reliance here.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a response to the Mu'tazila objection that tasdiq must be based on knowledge to be valid. The author first concedes the point for argument's sake, saying that even if that is the case, according to al-Ash'ari, such tasdiq still removes one from kufr and entitles one to reward. The author states that their goal is simply to establish the legal rulings of Islam (ahkam al-Islam), not to argue about the name. The name is secondary; what matters are the rulings. Then the author argues that whatever excuse the Mu'tazila use to establish the rulings (e.g., that tasdiq with knowledge suffices) can be used by us to apply the name. He substantiates: inference (istidlal) and acquiring knowledge are means to tasdiq, but when the objective is reached, the means are no longer required. Similarly, in the case of one seeking to repel a shubha—even without attained knowledge, he is a believer because his state (hal) of seeking makes him so. Thus, the same principle applies here: tasdiq itself, even without prior knowledge, suffices for the rulings.

أحكام الإسلام
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge is a necessary condition for the legal effects of faith.
Correction: The author shows that tasdiq (affirmation) itself suffices to establish the rulings; knowledge is only a means that can be absent without harm.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does tasdiq suffice even without prior knowledge?
A) Because inference and acquisition of knowledge are not commanded.
B) Because the objective is tasdiq, and once attained, the means are not necessary.
C) Because the Mu'tazila also allow it.
D) Because al-Ash'ari made it a condition.

Key Takeaway
Tasdiq itself, not necessarily preceded by knowledge, is sufficient to leave kufr and establish Islamic rulings; the means are subordinate to the objective.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Mu'tazila were inconsistent: they accepted tasdiq without knowledge in one case but not another. Are you consistent in how you judge others' faith? Do you hold yourself to a different standard? Commit today to applying the same mercy and logic to others that you apply to yourself.

The Self-Defeating Claim about Shubha and Kufr
Author refutes Mu'tazila claim about shubha causing kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that inability to repel a shubha causes kufr is self-defeating.

Core Idea
If shubha forces kufr, then every leader would be a kafir.

The Mu'tazila argued that being unable to repel a doubt (shubha) makes one a disbeliever, even if one seeks to repel it. The author responds with a powerful reductio ad absurdum: if this were true, then all people, especially their own leaders and imams, would have been guilty of kufr at some point. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
First, understand the position being refuted: that being unable to repel a shubha at the moment causes kufr, even if one is trying to repel it.
This is a key belief of the Mu'tazila on the nature of faith.
2
Identify the reductio
Notice how the author does not argue from scripture but from the logical consequences of their own claim. He shows that it would force them to admit kufr against themselves, their leaders, and all imams.
The argument proceeds in three stages: themselves, leaders, imams.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that since every leader has faced an irresolvable shubha and resorted to something impossible (muhal), by their own standard they would be considered kafir even by their own followers.
This makes the claim untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كابروا وقالوا: لما اعترضت الشبهة ولم يتمكن للحال من دفعها يكفر وإن كان في طلب دفعها، فقيل لهم: قد أقررتم على أنفسكم أولا، ثم على كل رئيس من رؤسائكم ثانيا، ثم على كل إمام كان في الأمة ثالثا، بالكفر وبطلان الأعمال مرارا إذ لا يخلو أحد من ذلك في عمره في الأحايين، ثم نقول لهم: ما من رئيس من رؤسائكم إلا وقد اعترضت له شبهة لم يتمكن من دفعها، وارتكب لأجلها محالا أكفره به جميع أهل نحلته فضلا عمن خالفهم فيكونون كلهم كفارا.
If they persist and say: When an ambiguity (shubha) confronts one and he is unable at that moment to repel it, he becomes a disbeliever even if he is seeking to repel it. Then it is said to them: You have admitted against yourselves first, then against every leader (ra'is) of your leaders second, then against every imam in the community third, disbelief and invalidity of deeds repeatedly, since no one is free from that in his lifetime at some times. Then we say to them: There is no leader among your leaders except that he was confronted by an ambiguity which he could not repel, and he committed for its sake something impossible (muhal), which all the people of his group considered him a disbeliever for, let alone those who differed with them; thus they would all be disbelievers.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The Mu'tazila make a further claim: if a person is confronted by a shubha and cannot repel it at that moment, he becomes a kafir (disbeliever) even if he is seeking to repel it. The author responds with a reductio ad absurdum. First, he says that this logic would force them to admit that every person, including themselves and their leaders, has been in such a state at some point, thus they would all be guilty of kufr at times. Then he specifies: every leader among them has faced a shubha he could not repel, and often resorted to an impossibility (muhal) in trying to resolve it, which even their own followers would consider kufr. Consequently, the Mu'tazila would have to regard themselves and their entire community as disbelievers. This shows the self-defeating nature of their claim.

إمام
محال
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person who is unable to repel a doubt (shubha) at a given moment becomes a disbeliever, even if he is trying to repel it.
Correction: This belief leads to absurd consequences, including that all people, especially their own leaders, would be disbelievers. Therefore, the claim is false.

D) The community would be split into believers and disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila claim that shubha causes kufr is self-refuting because it would imply their own leaders are disbelievers.

The Independent Existence of Knowledge and Tasdiq
Author proves knowledge and tasdiq can exist independently.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowledge (ilm) and assent (tasdiq) are distinct and can exist independently, using the examples of faith in the unseen and the obstinate recognition of truth.

Core Idea
Knowledge and assent are separate: one can exist without the other.

In this passage, the author addresses those who conflate knowledge and tasdiq by presenting two clear examples. He shows that believers affirm the existence of angels, Books, and Messengers without knowing their individual identities, while the obstinate (mu'anidun) recognize the truth but refuse to assent. This demonstrates the permissibility of their separation from both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's main argument and examples.
2
Identify the two proofs
The author provides two scenarios: one where tasdiq exists without knowledge, and one where knowledge exists without tasdiq. Note each example and reflect on how they support the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic citation
The author quotes Qur'an 6:20 about the People of the Book. Consider how this verse supports the claim that knowledge can exist without tasdiq.
4
Consider the theological implications
Think about why this distinction matters for debates on faith and disbelief, especially regarding the Mu'tazila and Jahm ibn Safwan.
This connects to broader sectarian discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: ليس من ضرورة انعدام العلم، انعدام التصديق، فإنا آمنا بالملائكة كلهم وكذا بالكتب والرسل ولا نعرفهم بأبدانهم، والمعاندون يعرفون ولا يصدقون على ما قال الله في علماء أهل الكتاب: يعْرِفُونَهُ كَمَا يَعْرِفُونَ أَبْنَاءَهُم فدل ذلك على جواز انفكاك التصديق عن العلم من الجانبين جميعا.
Then we say to them: It is not a necessary consequence of the absence of knowledge that tasdiq is absent. For we believe in all the angels, and likewise in the Books and Messengers, yet we do not know them by their bodies. And the obstinate (mu'anidun) know [the Prophet] but do not assent, as God said concerning the scholars of the People of the Book: 'They recognize him as they recognize their sons' (Qur'an 6:20). So that proves the permissibility of the separation of tasdiq from knowledge from both sides.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a proof that knowledge and tasdiq (assent) are distinct and can exist separately. The author argues from two directions. First, believers affirm (tasdiq) the existence of angels, Books, and Messengers without having detailed knowledge of their individual identities or bodies. Thus tasdiq exists without knowledge. Second, the obstinate (mu'anidun), like the Jewish scholars, recognized the Prophet Muhammad with knowledge (they knew him from their scriptures), yet they did not assent (does not have tasdiq). The author cites Quran 6:20 to support this: 'They recognize him as they recognize their sons.' So knowledge exists without tasdiq. Therefore, tasdiq and knowledge are separable. This refutes the Mu'tazila claim that tasdiq must be accompanied by knowledge at all times, and also rejects Jahm ibn Safwan's equation of iman with pure knowledge (ma'rifa).

Key Terms
علم
تصديق
ملائكة
كتب
رسل
معاندون
أهل الكتاب
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that belief (tasdiq) always requires detailed knowledge, or that knowledge always leads to belief.
Correction: The author shows that tasdiq can exist without knowledge (as in faith in the unseen), and knowledge can exist without tasdiq (as in the case of the obstinate).

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to show that tasdiq can exist without knowledge?
A) Belief in angels, Books, and Messengers without knowing them individually.
B) The Mu'tazila leaders who recognize truth but do not accept it.
C) The People of the Book who knew the Prophet but did not believe.
D) The committer of a major sin who has tasdiq but no knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and tasdiq are two distinct mental acts that can occur independently, as demonstrated by the examples of faith in the unseen and the obstinate recognition of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says believers assent to angels, Books, and Messengers without detailed knowledge. Is your faith in the unseen based on a genuine heart-assent, or is it merely intellectual acceptance? What would change in your life if your tasdiq deepened beyond just knowing?
?
Reflect on the phrase 'They recognize him as they recognize their sons'. This knowledge did not save the scholars of the People of the Book. Are there truths you recognize but have not fully embraced? What would it take for your recognition to transform into saving faith?

Affirmation Can Exist Without Knowledge
The author proves that knowledge and affirmation can be separate, using the example of angels and the People of the Book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how affirmation (tasdiq) can exist without detailed knowledge, and how knowledge can exist without affirmation, using examples from Islamic belief and the People of the Book.

Core Idea
Affirmation and knowledge can be separate; one does not require the other.

This lesson examines a key argument from Imam al-Nasafi about the relationship between knowledge and affirmation. The Mu'tazila claimed that faith requires detailed knowledge, but the author shows that this is not the case. Through two examples, he demonstrates that affirmation can exist without knowledge, and knowledge can exist without affirmation. This is crucial for understanding the nature of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing. Then read the translation to capture the argument's flow.
Notice the author uses two contrasting examples.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two scenarios: Muslims affirming angels without knowing them, and People of the Book knowing the truth but not affirming. Identify each and note the difference.
3
Reflect on the implication for faith
Ask yourself: If affirmation can exist without full knowledge, what does that mean for the nature of iman? How does this refute the Mu'tazila position?
This connects to the broader debate about the definition of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم : ليس من ضرورة انعدام العلم، انعدام التصديق، فإنا آمنا بالملائكة كلهم وكذا بالكتب والرسل ولا نعرفهم بأبدانهم، والمعاندون يعرفون ولا يصدقون على ما قال الله في علماء أهل الكتاب : يعرفونه كما يعرفون أبناءهم فدل ذلك على جواز انفكاك التصديق عن العلم من الجانبين جميعا
Then we say to them: It is not necessary that absence of knowledge entails absence of affirmation. For we believe in all the angels, and likewise in the books and messengers, even though we do not know them in their bodies. And the obstinate ones know but do not affirm, as God said concerning the scholars of the People of the Book: 'They recognize it as they recognize their sons' (6:20). So that indicates the permissibility of the separation of affirmation from knowledge from both sides.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage provides a separate proof. The author argues that lack of knowledge does not necessarily imply lack of affirmation (tasdiq). He gives two examples: Muslims affirm the existence of angels, books, and messengers without having direct knowledge of their physical forms. Conversely, the People of the Book recognized the truth about the Prophet Muhammad (as described in their scriptures) but did not affirm it. This shows that affirmation can exist without detailed knowledge, and knowledge can exist without affirmation. Thus, the Mu'tazila's insistence on knowledge as a condition for faith is refuted.

Key Terms

علم
ملائكة
أهل الكتاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief (iman) requires detailed intellectual knowledge of the object of belief.
Correction: Affirmation of the heart (tasdiq) can exist without detailed knowledge, as shown by belief in angels. Conversely, knowledge can exist without affirmation, as with the People of the Book.

Checkpoint

B) That affirmation can exist without detailed knowledge

D) That knowledge and affirmation are identical

Key Takeaway
Affirmation (tasdiq) does not require detailed knowledge; it is a commitment of the heart that can coexist with or without full intellectual understanding.

The Muqallid as a Scholar: Implicit Proof
Argument that muqallid is a scholar via implicit proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the muqallid (blind follower) can be considered a scholar because his belief is based on an implicit proof, even without explicit demonstrative reasoning.

Core Idea
Even blind faith rests on some proof, making the follower a scholar.

This lesson explores a fascinating defense of the muqallid's status. Some scholars argued that the muqallid is not merely a blind follower but actually a scholar because his acceptance of faith is grounded in a proof—either the perceived trustworthiness of the person who invited him or the evident merits of the religion itself. This implicit proof, combined with certainty, elevates him to the rank of a scholar. Let's examine this argument as presented in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument. Note that the text is incomplete at the end.
The passage ends abruptly, so focus on the complete portion.
2
Identify the two bases for implicit proof
The text mentions two ways the muqallid arrives at proof: from the trustworthiness of the caller (who must not be a liar) or from observing the merits of the religion itself. Notice how each serves as a foundation for belief.
These are the key components that make the muqallid a scholar.
3
Understand why this makes him a scholar
Even though each individual indication is probabilistic, when the muqallid builds his belief on it without doubting its falsehood, and the belief matches reality, he is considered to have knowledge. Reflect on this reasoning.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The text cuts off with a phrase that likely applies this ruling to a specific scenario. Think about how the argument might be extended or qualified.
This part is unclear due to the incomplete text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هو عالم لأنه وإن لم يكن عالما بدليل لكن لا شك أن الإنسان لا يترك دينه الذي هو أعز عنده من نفسه وماله وأهله وولده إلا بضرب دليل يبدو له إما من جهة هذا الذي دعاه إلى دين أنه لا يكذب أو من جهة الدين يرى من محاسنه فيبني اعتقاده عليه وهو يصلح في الجملة دليلا، وإن كان الواحد محتملا ولكنه لما بنى اعتقاده عليه ولم يخطر بباله جواز كونه كاذبا وكان المعتقد كما اعتقد، فأنزل عالما، ثم نزل هذه المسألة في حق من كان لما خبر
And some of them said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar through a proof, there is no doubt that a person does not leave his religion, which is dearer to him than his life, wealth, family, and children, except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the aspect that the one who called him to the religion does not lie, or from the aspect that he sees the merits of the religion, so he builds his belief upon it. And it is in general a proof, even if any single (indication) is probabilistic. But when he builds his belief upon it and does not conceive the possibility of it being false, and the believed thing is as he believed, then he is considered a scholar. Then he applied this issue concerning one who was... for what came.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative view. Some scholars argue that the muqallid is actually a scholar (عالم) because his belief is based on a proof, even if it is not an explicit demonstrative proof. The proof could be the perceived trustworthiness of the person who invited him to Islam, or the apparent merits of the religion itself. Although each individual indication may be probabilistic, when combined with the muqallid's certainty and lack of doubt about its falsehood, it becomes a sufficient basis for knowledge. Therefore, he is considered knowledgeable. The text then cuts off, mentioning that this issue was applied to someone who ... (incomplete).

Correction: This passage shows that some scholars argue the muqallid's belief is based on a proof, even if implicit, such as trust in the caller or the merits of the religion.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid can be considered a scholar because his belief is founded on an implicit proof, such as trust in the caller or the religion's merits, combined with certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

And what proof—implicit or explicit—kept you from leaving? If you have never questioned, does that mean your faith is strong, or have you simply avoided the hard questions?

The Muqallid's Implicit Proof Makes Him a Scholar
Presents the argument that the muqallid is a scholar and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars consider a muqallid to be a scholar based on the implicit proof he holds.

Core Idea
Implicit trust in a truthful caller is itself a form of proof.

This lesson examines a second opinion on the muqallid: that he is actually a scholar because he does not abandon his religion without some reason, even if not formally articulated. We'll see how this reasoning applies to a person raised in isolation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: general principle then example.
2
Identify the key argument
The passage argues that a person does not leave his religion except with some kind of proof. Find that statement and see how it defines 'proof' broadly.
3
Apply to the isolated individual
The author gives an example of someone raised in isolation who accepts Islam after meeting a Muslim. Consider whether his acceptance counts as proof-based.
This is the point of disagreement among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال : هو عالم لأنه وإن لم يكن عالما بدليل لكن لا شك أن الإنسان لا يترك دينه الذي هو أعز عنده من نفسه وماله وأهله وولده إلا بضرب دليل يبدو له إما من جهة هذا الذي دعاه إلى دين أنه لا يكذب أو من جهة الدين يرى من محاسنه فيبني اعتقاده عليه وهو يصلح في الجملة دليلا، وإن كان الواحد محتملا ولكنه لما بنى اعتقاده عليه ولم يخطر بباله جواز كونه كاذبا وكان المعتقد كما اعتقد، فأنزل عالما، ثم نزل هذه المسألة في حق من كان ينشأ في قطر من الأقطار، أو شاهق جبل من الجبال، لم تبلغه الدعوى ولا علم بثبوت هذه الملة، فشاهده مسلم فدعاه إلى الدين، وبين له ما يجب اعتقاده، وأُخْبِرَ رسولا لنا بلغ هذا الدين عن الله ودعانا إليه وقد ظهرت على يده المعجزات الناقضات للعادة، فصدقه هذا الإنسان في جميع ذلك واعتقد الدين من غير سابقة تأمل وتفكر هذا هو الذي اختلفوا فيه، فأما أهل دار الإسلام عوامهم وخواصهم نسوانهم وصبيانهم، العاقلون أهل الأمصار والرساتيق والقرى، وسكان الصحاري والبراري فكلهم مؤمنون مسلمون عارفون الله تعالى ووحدانيته وغير ذلك لن يخلو أحد منهم عن ضرب الاستدلال، وإن كان لا يهتدي إلى العبارة عن دليله، ولا يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة حتى إن واحدا منهم متى عاين هؤلاء من الأهوال كرعد هائل وهبوب ريح عاصفة أو ظلمة راكدة يسبح للحال، ويصف الله تعالى بكمال القدرة ونفاذ المشيئة وتمام العلم والحكمة علما منه أن لا تعلق لهذه الأفزاع إلا بقدرته التامة ومشيئته النفاذة، وهو الذي خلق السماوات بغير عمد ممدودة وأطناب مشدودة، وجعل فيها الأفلاك الدائرة، والنجوم السائرة، وخلق الأرض وجعل فيها الجبال الواسعة، وشق فيها الأنهار الجارية، على هذا أهل جميع الأسواق والنواحي والقرى والرساتيق والنسوان والعقلاء من الصبيان، فلم يكن في هؤلاء بين مشايخنا خلاف ولا بيننا وبين الأشعري، وإنما الخلاف فيهم بيننا وبين المعتزلة على ما مر، والحمد لله على التوفيق والهداية.
And among them is one who said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar with a proof, there is no doubt that a man does not abandon his religion—which is dearer to him than himself, his wealth, his family, and his child—except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the side of the one who called him to the religion, that he does not lie, or from the side of the religion, he sees its excellences and builds his belief upon it, and that is in general suitable as a proof, even if individually it is probable. But when he built his belief upon it and did not conceive the possibility of his being a liar, and the object of belief was as he believed, then he is considered a scholar. Then he applied this issue to one who grows up in a region of regions, or on a high mountain of mountains, to whom the call has not reached nor does he know of the establishment of this creed. Then a Muslim saw him, called him to the religion, and explained to him what he must believe, and he was informed that a messenger of ours conveyed this religion from God and called us to it, and miracles breaking custom appeared at his hand. So this human being believed him in all that and believed the religion without prior reflection and thought. This is that which they differed over. As for the people of the Abode of Islam, their commoners and their elite, their women and their children, the rational ones among the people of the cities, the rural districts, and the villages, and the inhabitants of deserts and wildernesses—all of them are believers, Muslims, knowers of God Most High and His Oneness and other matters. Not one of them is free from a kind of inference, even if he cannot articulate his proof nor is able to repel the objections that arise. So much so that if one of them witnesses among these horrors—a tremendous thunder, a violent wind, or a dense darkness—he immediately glorifies God and describes God Most High with perfect power, effective will, complete knowledge and wisdom, knowing that these terrors are connected only to His perfect power and effective will. And He is the One who created the heavens without extended supports or tightened ropes, and placed in them the rotating spheres and the traveling stars, and created the earth and placed in it the vast mountains and split in it the flowing rivers. Upon this are all the people of the markets, regions, villages, rural districts, women, and the rational ones among children. So there was no disagreement among our masters concerning these people, nor between us and al-Ash'ari. Rather, the disagreement concerning them is between us and the Mu'tazila, as has passed. And praise be to God for success and guidance.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a second opinion on the muqallid: some scholars say he is actually a scholar (''alim'). The argument is that even without an explicit formal proof, a person does not abandon his religion—which he holds dearer than life—without some kind of reason. This reason may be trust in the truthfulness of the caller or appreciation of the religion's virtues. Although such reasoning may be merely probable, it still provides a basis for belief, and if the person does not doubt it, he is considered a scholar. The author then applies this to a specific case: a person raised in isolation who meets a Muslim, learns about Islam, and accepts it after hearing about the Prophet's miracles. This case is the one scholars differed over regarding whether such a person is a scholar. The author then asserts a broad consensus: all Muslims within the Islamic realm—commoners, elites, women, children, urban and rural—are believers and knowers of God, because none of them is entirely devoid of some form of inference (istidlal), even if they cannot articulate it. As evidence, the author describes how even a simple person, upon witnessing a natural phenomenon like thunder, automatically attributes it to God's power and wisdom, which is an implicit inference. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris on this matter; the only disagreement is with the Mu'tazila, who presumably held that only those with explicit demonstrative knowledge are true believers.

Key Terms
المقلد
العالم
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only scholars with formal demonstrative proofs have true faith and can be considered 'alim.
Correction: This passage shows that implicit trust in a truthful caller or the religion's virtues, when held without doubt, constitutes a form of proof that qualifies the person as a scholar.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do some scholars consider the muqallid to be a scholar?
A) Because he can recite the Quran perfectly.
B) Because he never abandons his religion except with some kind of proof, even if implicit.
C) Because he performs all religious obligations without question.
D) Because he has memorized the proofs of theologians.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid can be considered a scholar because he holds an implicit proof, even if not articulated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage says a man does not abandon his religion except for a reason. Has there ever been a moment when you doubted your faith? What was the proof that kept you anchored? If not, are you aware that the strength of your faith may have never been tested?

Universal Istidlal: All Muslims Have Implicit Inference
Shows that all Muslims have istidlal and there is consensus with Ash'aris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all Muslims, even commoners, possess a form of rational inference (istidlal) in their faith.

Core Idea
Even the simplest person infers God's power from natural phenomena.

After discussing the muqallid, the author broadens the scope to show that all Muslims within the Islamic realm are true believers because they all engage in some form of inference, whether articulate or not. The example of someone witnessing thunder demonstrates this innate reasoning.

And among them is one who said: He is a scholar, because even if he is not a scholar with a proof, there is no doubt that a man does not abandon his religion—which is dearer to him than himself, his wealth, his family, and his child—except by a kind of proof that appears to him, either from the side of the one who called him to the religion, that he does not lie, or from the side of the religion, he sees its excellences and builds his belief upon it, and that is in general suitable as a proof, even if individually it is probable. Upon this are all the people of the markets, regions, villages, rural districts, women, and the rational ones among children. So there was no disagreement among our masters concerning these people, nor between us and al-Ash'ari. Rather, the disagreement concerning them is between us and the Mu'tazila, as has passed. And praise be to God for success and guidance.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents a second opinion on the muqallid: some scholars say he is actually a scholar (''alim'). The argument is that even without an explicit formal proof, a person does not abandon his religion—which he holds dearer than life—without some kind of reason. This reason may be trust in the truthfulness of the caller or appreciation of the religion's virtues. Although such reasoning may be merely probable, it still provides a basis for belief, and if the person does not doubt it, he is considered a scholar. The author then applies this to a specific case: a person raised in isolation who meets a Muslim, learns about Islam, and accepts it after hearing about the Prophet's miracles. This case is the one scholars differed over regarding whether such a person is a scholar. The author then asserts a broad consensus: all Muslims within the Islamic realm—commoners, elites, women, children, urban and rural—are believers and knowers of God, because none of them is entirely devoid of some form of inference (istidlal), even if they cannot articulate it. As evidence, the author describes how even a simple person, upon witnessing a natural phenomenon like thunder, automatically attributes it to God's power and wisdom, which is an implicit inference. The passage concludes that there is no disagreement between the Maturidis and the Ash'aris on this matter; the only disagreement is with the Mu'tazila, who presumably held that only those with explicit demonstrative knowledge are true believers.

Key Terms
الاستدلال
المعجزات الناقضات للعادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only scholars who can articulate formal proofs are true believers and have knowledge of God.
Correction: This passage shows that all Muslims, including commoners and children, engage in implicit inference from natural phenomena, which suffices for true belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who among the following is included in the universal claim that all Muslims have istidlal?
A) Only the scholars and elites.
B) Only the men who live in cities.
C) All Muslims: commoners, elite, women, children, urban and rural.
D) Only those who can articulate their proofs.

Key Takeaway
All Muslims, regardless of station, have a basic rational inference embedded in their faith, confirming the universality of istidlal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden terrifying event like a thunderstorm or earthquake, what is your immediate inner reaction? Does your heart turn to God spontaneously, or do you only think of natural causes? This lesson says even a simple person instinctively glorifies God—examine whether your own reaction aligns with that innate fitrah.
?
Are you ever ashamed that you cannot defend your faith with sophisticated philosophical arguments like the scholars? This lesson teaches that even if you cannot articulate proof, you already have implicit inference. How does this reality change the way you view your own faith and that of others who may be less educated?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who seems simple or uneducated but has deep devotion. Based on this lesson, how do you think God views their faith? Are you tempted to look down on them? Let this passage correct your heart and fill you with respect for the universal gift of istidlal.

Faith and Disbelief
