Introduction to Names and Rulings
Introduces the new topic of how to name and judge major sinners among Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central question of the Islamic theological debate on the status of major sinners among Muslims.

Core Idea
The debate on 'Names and Rulings' asks: what do we call a Muslim who commits a major sin, and what is their fate?

This passage opens a new section of the text, marking a shift from broader theological foundations to a specific issue that divided early Islamic sects. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, frames the debate by stating that people disagreed about both the name and the final judgment of those who commit major sins. As you read, note that the passage only introduces the problem; it does not yet provide the Maturidi answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read both the Arabic and translation to grasp the topic. Note the key terms: 'al-asma' wa al-ahkam' (names and rulings) and 'al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (promise and threat).
The Arabic is concise; the translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the two questions
The passage asks two things: by what name are major sinners called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife. This is the core of the debate.
3
Understand the context
Recognize that this topic is historically contentious between Kharijites, Mu'tazila, and Ahl al-Sunna. The passage sets up the Maturidi position which will be elaborated later.
This is just the introduction; answers come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد. اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟
Discussion on Names and Rulings (al-asma' wa al-ahkam) and Promise and Threat (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id). People differed regarding those who commit major sins (kaba'ir) among the people of the Qibla: by what name are they called, and what is their judgment in the afterlife?
Source pages p. 1042

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new major section in the book. The author announces the topic: 'al-asma' wa al-ahkam wa al-wa'd wa al-wa'id' (Names and Rulings, and Promise and Threat). This is a classic theological discussion about the status of Muslims who commit major sins (al-kaba'ir). The central question is twofold: (1) What term should be used for such individuals? Are they still believers (mu'minun), or do they become sinners (fussaq), or something else? (2) What will be their final judgment in the Hereafter? Will they be punished eternally or temporarily? The author notes that people (i.e., different Islamic sects) have disagreed on this. This sets up the subsequent elaboration of the Maturidi position, which will be distinguished from the Kharijites (who deem grave sinners as unbelievers), the Mu'tazila (who place them in a middle position), and others.

Key Terms

الوعد والوعيد (al-wa'd wa al-wa'id)
الكبائر (al-kaba'ir)
أهل القبلة (ahl al-qiblah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

The debate on Names and Rulings concerns the naming and ultimate fate of Muslims who commit major sins.

?
The phrase 'People of the Qibla' reminds us that outward acts of worship do not guarantee that judgment is easy. How often do you take your prayers for granted? What would it mean to you if your prayer was the very thing that keeps you in the fold of Islam even when you fall into sin?

Orthodox View on Major Sinners and Their Fate
Al-Nasafi presents the orthodox Sunni view on major sinners and their fate in the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the orthodox Sunni position on the naming and ultimate destiny of believers who commit major sins, including the role of God's will, intercession, and the meaning of irja'.

Core Idea
Major sinners remain believers; their final fate is in God's hands, but they ultimately enter Paradise.

This lesson examines the classical Sunni response to a critical theological question: what happens to a Muslim who commits a major sin? Al-Nasafi presents the balanced view of Ahl al-Haqq, affirming both the sinner's retained faith and the possibility of punishment, while ultimately securing Paradise. The passage also clarifies the misunderstood label of 'Murji'' applied to Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Understand the theological question
The passage opens by stating the disagreement: what to call those who commit major sins among the people of the Qibla, and what is their judgment in the Hereafter? Keep this question in mind.
2
Learn the definition of the sinner
Ahl al-Haqq defines the person as one who commits a major sin without considering it lawful or belittling it, due to desire or zeal, hoping for forgiveness and fearing punishment. This person is still called a believer.
The key is that faith is not removed by the sin.
3
Grasp the judgment in the Hereafter
If such a person dies without repentance, God has discretion (mashi'a): He may forgive by His bounty or through intercession, or He may punish according to the sin. But the final outcome is Paradise, not eternal Hell.
This balances mercy and justice.
4
Clarify the 'Murji'' label
The passage explains why Abu Hanifa was called a Murji': because he deferred judgment to God's will. It then shows that this deferment is rooted in the angels' humble statement in the Quran.
Irja' means deferment, not a denial of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في الأسماء والأحكام والوعد والوعيد. اختلف الناس في مقترفي الكبائر من أهل القبلة أنهم بماذا يسمون وأن حكمهم في الآخرة ما هو؟ قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد من الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا
Discussion on names, judgments, promise, and threat. People differed concerning those among the people of the Qibla who commit major sins: by what name are they called, and what is their judgment in the Hereafter? The People of Truth said: Whoever commits a major sin without considering it lawful and without belittling it, due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that God will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him, then this one is called a believer. He remains upon what he was of faith; his faith has not left him nor has it been invalidated. And no one leaves faith except through the door by which he entered. His judgment is that if he dies without repentance, God has discretion concerning him: if He wills, He pardons him by His bounty and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds, or by the intercession of some of the righteous; and if He wills, He punishes him according to his sin, then his final outcome is Paradise without doubt, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. And Abu Hanifa - may God have mercy on him - was called a Murji' because he deferred the matter of the grave sinner to God's will, and 'irja'' means deferment. And it is reported from him that he was asked: From whom did you take irja'? He said: From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.'
Source pages p. 1042

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the orthodox Sunni position on the naming and ultimate fate of Muslims who commit major sins. It begins with the heading 'Discussion on Names, Judgments, Promise, and Threat' and states the point of contention: what to call such people and what their final judgment is. Then it gives the view of Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth). It defines the person as one who commits a major sin without istihlal (deeming it lawful) or istikhfaf (belittling it), but due to overwhelming desire or zeal, and who fears God's punishment and hopes for His mercy. This person remains a believer; his faith is not removed. He only exits faith through the same door he entered, meaning by renouncing belief itself. The judgment for such a person who dies unrepentant is that God has the will (mashi'a) to either forgive him out of His bounty or to punish him according to his sin, but his final destination is Paradise, and he will not abide eternally in Hell. The passage also addresses the labeling of Abu Hanifa as a Murji' (one who postpones judgment) because he deferred the fate of the grave sinner to God's will, explaining that 'irja' means deferment. Abu Hanifa traced this deferment to the angels' statement in the Quran (2:32), showing that it is a humble acknowledgment of limited knowledge.

Key Terms
الكبيرة
الإيمان
المشيئة
الشفاعة
الخلود
الإرجاء
أبو حنيفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the orthodox view is identical to the Murji'a position of no punishment at all.
Correction: The passage clearly states that punishment is possible under God's will, though the final outcome is Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the fate of a Muslim who dies without repentance after committing a major sin?
A) He will be eternally in Hell
B) He is subject to God's will and may be forgiven or punished but ultimately enters Paradise
C) He is considered a hypocrite and will be in the lowest depths of Hell
D) He is no longer a believer and will be in Hell forever

Key Takeaway
A believer's major sin does not negate faith; God has discretion, but the ultimate outcome is Paradise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Evil Murji'a View on Major Sins
Al-Nasafi presents and refutes the extreme Murji'ite claim that no Muslim is punished for any major sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme Murji'ite position that faith renders major sins harmless, and why it is condemned as contrary to orthodoxy.

Core Idea
Faith does not make sin harmless; the evil Murji'a err by claiming it does.

Al-Nasafi wastes no time in condemning a radical sectarian view: that a Muslim who commits major sins will never be punished because faith itself neutralizes evil deeds. This was the claim of the extreme Murji'a, attributed to the commentator Muqatil ibn Sulayman. Understanding this position helps clarify the orthodox Ahl al-Sunna stance on sin and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the exact wording of the Murji'ite claim.
Notice the strong language: 'evil Murji'a' indicates this is a rejected view.
2
Analyze the analogy
Identify the two-part analogy: good deeds useless with disbelief, evil deeds harmless with faith. Understand why this analogy is flawed from an orthodox perspective.
3
Consider the source attribution
Reflect on why Al-Nasafi names Muqatil ibn Sulayman. This is often done to discredit the opinion by linking it to a specific figure.
Muqatil's tafsir was influential but his views were criticized.
4
Contrast with orthodox belief
Recall or infer the Ahl al-Sunna position: major sins are dangerous and may lead to punishment unless repented, though faith prevents eternal damnation.
This will be covered in more detail in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير.
And the evil Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims will be punished for any of the major sins, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so an evil deed does not harm with faith. This opinion is reported from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the author of the Tafsir.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This brief passage presents the extreme Murji'ite position, which is contrary to the orthodox view. The author labels them as 'evil' (khabitha). Their claim is twofold: first, that no Muslim will suffer punishment for any major sin; second, they draw an analogy: just as good deeds are useless alongside disbelief, so evil deeds are harmless alongside faith. In other words, faith renders sin ineffective. The author attributes this view to Muqatil ibn Sulayman, known for his tafsir, implying that this is an erroneous opinion.

Correction: The text specifically targets the 'evil Murji'a' who claim no punishment at all, not all Murji'a.

Mu'tazilite Doctrine of the Intermediate State
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite position on the intermediate state and eternal punishment for major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite position that a major sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever but occupies an intermediate state, and that such a person will be eternally punished in Hell if unrepentant.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny the label of believer to grave sinners, placing them in a middle category with eternal punishment.

The Mu'tazilite school of theology held distinctive views on the status of those who commit major sins. In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi summarizes their position: such a person is not a believer, not a disbeliever, but a fasiq in an intermediate station. Their fate, if they die without repentance, is eternal punishment in Hell. This view contrasts sharply with orthodox Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three distinct claims: (1) the label of the grave sinner is 'fasiq', not believer or disbeliever; (2) such a person has an intermediate station between faith and disbelief; (3) if he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in Hell and his faith will not help.
Notice the logical sequence: naming, status, consequence.
3
Contrast with the orthodox view
Reflect on how this differs from the standard Sunni position that a grave sinner remains a believer and is subject to God's will—possible forgiveness or temporary punishment.
This comparison will be elaborated in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه
And the Mu'tazila said: The name of this one is fasiq; he is neither called a believer nor a disbeliever. And he has an intermediate station between the two stations of faith and disbelief. And his judgment is that if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith will not benefit him.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This brief passage outlines the Mu'tazilite position on the naming and fate of the grave sinner. They assign him the label fasiq, rejecting both the labels of believer and disbeliever. This is their famous doctrine of 'the intermediate position' (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). Unlike the orthodox view that he remains a believer and is subject to God's will, the Mu'tazila assert that if he dies unrepentant, he will be eternally in Hell, and his faith is of no avail. This is a direct contrast to the orthodox view of possible forgiveness and eventual Paradise.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الفسق
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
الخلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite 'intermediate position' is similar to the orthodox view that a grave sinner is still a believer with sin.
Correction: In fact, the Mu'tazila deny the label of believer entirely to the grave sinner and assign him an intermediate status, which is distinct from the orthodox position.

Checkpoint

B) He will be punished temporarily and then enter Paradise
C) He will abide eternally in Hell
D) He will be in a state of limbo

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state who, if unrepentant, will suffer eternal punishment in Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly rely on your faith alone to save you, regardless of unrepented sins? The Mu'tazila said faith cannot benefit an unrepentant sinner—are you certain that your trust in God's mercy is not presumption?
?
If God were to judge you this moment, would you be content to be called a 'fasiq' rather than a 'believer'? This lesson strips away comfortable labels—what label truly fits your inner state?
?
The Mu'tazila made the grave sinner an in-between category. But in your heart, do you believe your own sins place you closer to the disbelievers than to the prophets? How does that belief affect your daily choices?

The Grave Sinner: Believer and His Ultimate Fate
Ahl al-Haqq define the grave sinner and his final fate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of a grave sinner as a believer and the conditional nature of his punishment.

Core Idea
A grave sinner remains a believer; his final home is Paradise.

This lesson presents the core teaching of Ahl al-Haqq regarding those who commit major sins. It clarifies that such a person is not stripped of faith, and his ultimate fate rests with Allah's mercy and justice. The passage establishes a balanced position between despair and presumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the precise conditions given for the grave sinner.
2
Identify the definition and conditions
Find the description of the grave sinner: what he does not do (deem lawful, treat lightly), why he sins (overwhelming desire/zeal), and his attitude (hope and fear).
These conditions are crucial for the ruling.
3
Understand the ruling on his fate
Examine the statement about his status as a believer and the options after death: forgiveness, intercession, or punishment, but ultimate Paradise.
This contrasts with both extremists who guarantee hell and those who guarantee paradise without repentance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رأي أهل الحق
قال أهل الحق: إن من اقترف كبيرة غير مستحل لها ولا مستخف بها لغلبة شهوة أو حمية يرجو الله تعالى أن يغفر له ويخاف أن يعذبه فهذا اسمه مؤمن، بقي على ما كان عليه من الإيمان لم يزل عنه إيمانه ولم ينتقض، ولا يخرج أحد الإيمان إلا من الباب الذي دخل فيه، فحكمه أنه لو مات من غير توبة فلله فيه المشيئة إن شاء عفا عنه بفضله وكرمه أو يتركه وما معه من الإيمان والحسنات أو بشفاعة بعض الأخيار، وإن شاء عذبه بقدر ذنبه ثم عاقبة أمره الجنة لا محالة ولا يخلد في النار، وكان أبو حنيفة رحمه الله يسمى مرجئًا لتأخيره أمر صاحب الكبيرة إلى مشيئة الله تعالى، والإرجاء هو التأخير، وروى عنه أنه قيل له ممن أخذت الإرجاء، فقال: من الملائكة عليهم السلام حيث قالوا: لَا عِلْمَ لَنَا إِلَّا مَا عَلَّمْتَنَا وزعمت المرجئة الخبيثة أن أحدًا من المسلمين لا يعاقب على شيء من الكبائر، وكما أن الحسنة لا تنفع مع الكفر فالسيئة لا تضر مع الإيمان حكى هذا القول عن مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير.
The view of Ahl al-Haqq: Ahl al-Haqq said: Whoever commits a major sin without deeming it lawful nor treating it lightly due to overwhelming desire or zeal, hoping that Allah will forgive him and fearing that He will punish him, then his name is a believer. He remains upon what he was upon of faith; his faith has not left him nor is it invalidated. No one leaves faith except through the door by which he entered. Its ruling: if he dies without repentance, then concerning him is the will of Allah. If He wills, He forgives him by His bounty and generosity, or He leaves him with what he has of faith and good deeds, or through the intercession of some righteous ones. If He wills, He punishes him according to his sin; then the final outcome of his affair is Paradise undoubtedly, and he will not abide eternally in the Fire. Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, was called Murji' due to deferring the matter of the grave sinner to the will of Allah. Al-Irja' is deferment. It is narrated that he was asked: From whom did you take irja'? He said: From the angels, peace be upon them, when they said: 'We have no knowledge except what You have taught us.' The evil Murji'a claimed that none of the Muslims will be punished for any major sin, and just as a good deed does not benefit with disbelief, so a sin does not harm with faith. This statement is narrated from Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the commentator.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Ahl al-Haqq (the People of Truth, i.e., Sunni Muslims) regarding the grave sinner (sāḥib al-kabīra). It defines the grave sinner as one who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful or being disdainful, but rather due to overpowering desire or zeal, while still hoping for forgiveness and fearing punishment. Such a person remains a believer; his faith is not nullified. The ruling for one who dies without repentance is that his ultimate fate is left to God's will: He may forgive by His grace, accept intercession, or punish proportionally, but the final destination is Paradise, not eternal hellfire. The passage then addresses the label 'Murji'a' applied to Abu Hanifa due to his deferring judgment to God's will. Abu Hanifa refutes the extremist Murji'a who claim that sin does not harm faith, attributing that view to Muqatil ibn Sulayman. The passage thus clarifies the orthodox position against both the extremists and their accusers.

Key Terms
كبيرة
مؤمن
إيمان
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Haqq guarantee Paradise for all grave sinners regardless of repentance.
Correction: The passage states that the final outcome is Paradise, but it also mentions the possibility of punishment according to the sin. Repentance is not mentioned here, but punishment is a real possibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the ultimate outcome for a grave sinner who dies without repentance?
A) Eternal hellfire
B) Paradise undoubtedly
C) He remains in a state between faith and disbelief
D) He is a hypocrite in the lowest depths of hell

Key Takeaway
A Muslim who commits a major sin without deeming it lawful remains a believer, and his final destination is Paradise, though he may be punished first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly fear Allah’s punishment and hope for His forgiveness as this passage describes, or have you become desensitized to sin? What will you do today to revive that fear and hope?
?
This lesson says that even a grave sinner may be punished before entering Paradise. Are you living as though you are immune to that punishment, assuming your faith alone guarantees immediate paradise? What would change if you took that possibility seriously?
?
The passage mentions intercession as a means of forgiveness for grave sinners. Do you rely more on the intercession of others than on your own repentance and turning back to Allah? How can you balance hope in intercession with personal accountability?

The Murji'a Label and Extremist Views
Abu Hanifa and the Murji'a dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origin of the label 'Murji'a' applied to Abu Hanifa and the refutation of extremist Murji'a views.

Core Idea
Irja' means deferring judgment to Allah, not denying sin's harm.

This lesson addresses the historical context of the term 'Murji'a'. It shows how Abu Hanifa was called Murji'a because he left the fate of the grave sinner to God's will, and how he derived this concept from the angels. It also refutes the extremist claim that sin does not harm faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Note the shift from the general ruling to the historical controversy.
2
Understand the meaning of irja'
Find the definition of irja' given in the passage and how Abu Hanifa explained its origin.
Irja' comes from the angels' statement in the Quran.
3
Identify the extremist view and its refutation
Look for the claim of the evil Murji'a and the attribution to Muqatil ibn Sulayman. Understand why it is rejected.
They claimed sin does not harm faith, which contradicts the earlier ruling.

The Grave Sinner's Station: Mu'tazila Doctrine
Mu'tazila define the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila concept of the intermediate station (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn) for the grave sinner and its consequences.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila place the grave sinner between faith and disbelief, but with a severe eternal fate.

The Mu'tazila, an early theological school, introduced a unique category for those who commit major sins but still profess faith. Unlike the Kharijites who deem them disbelievers and the Murji'ah who consider them believers, the Mu'tazila assign them an intermediate status. This passage outlines their position and its implications for the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the three key claims: the naming (fasiq), the station (manzila), and the ruling (hukm).
Notice that the passage explicitly says 'not called a believer nor a disbeliever'.
2
Identify the terminology
Identify the three terms: 'fasiq', 'manzila bayn al-manzilatayn', and 'khuld fi al-nar'. Understand each term's significance.
3
Reflect on the logical consequence
Consider why the Mu'tazila argue that forgiveness after death without repentance is not from wisdom. This reflects their view on divine justice.
This step connects the passage to broader Mu'tazila theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه وطاعته وليس من الحكمة عفوه ومغفرته، وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.
The Mu'tazila said: The name of this person is a disobedient sinner (fāsiq); he is not called a believer nor a disbeliever. He has a station between the station of faith and disbelief. Its ruling: if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith and obedience will not benefit him, and it is not from wisdom to pardon him and forgive him. As for the committer of a minor sin, he is in their view a believer, and if he avoids major sins he deserves forgiveness for minor sins, and it is not permissible that Allah punish him for them.
Source pages p. 1043

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila doctrine on the grave sinner. They label him a fāsiq (disobedient sinner) and assign him a status between belief and unbelief (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). His ruling if he dies without repentance is eternal hellfire, and his faith does not save him. They also hold that God cannot forgive him because it would contradict wisdom. For minor sins, the person remains a believer and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness and God cannot punish him for them.

Key Terms
فاسق
منزلة بين منزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila consider the grave sinner a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage explicitly states he is not called a disbeliever (kafir), but rather a fasiq in an intermediate state.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the grave sinner in Mu'tazila theology?
A) He is a disbeliever (kafir)
B) He is a believer (mu'min)
C) He is a fasiq in an intermediate station
D) He is a hypocrite (munafiq)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila categorize the grave sinner as a fasiq in an intermediate state, with eternal punishment if unrepentant, reflecting their strict view of divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you died tonight with a major sin you never repented from, would you feel you deserve eternal punishment? How does the Mu'tazila insistence on justice without 'easy forgiveness' challenge your assumptions about God's mercy? Do you need to examine your view of sin?
?
The Mu'tazila claim it is 'not from wisdom' for God to pardon an unrepentant grave sinner. Do you think God's wisdom could ever allow forgiveness without repentance? What does your answer reveal about how you balance hope in mercy and fear of justice?
?
Imagine you are the grave sinner described: you have faith and good deeds, but you die with a major sin unrepented. According to this teaching, your faith and obedience 'will not benefit you.' If that were true, would you still consider yourself safe? What does that tell you about the urgency of repentance right now?

Minor Sins According to the Mu'tazila
Mu'tazila hold that the committer of a minor sin remains a believer and deserves forgiveness if avoiding major sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila view on minor sins and how they differ from major sins in their theology.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not remove one from faith if major sins are avoided; forgiveness is deserved.

In contrast to their strict stance on major sins, the Mu'tazila maintain that minor sins are forgivable and do not affect one's status as a believer, provided one avoids major sins. This passage completes the picture of their theology of sin and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the key condition: 'if he avoids major sins'.
2
Analyze the condition and consequence
Identify what the Mu'tazila say about the minor sinner's faith and the deserved forgiveness. Compare with the previous lesson on major sins.
Notice the phrase 'لا يجوز' – it is not permissible for Allah to punish, showing an obligation on God's part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : إن اسم هذا فاسق ولا يسمى مؤمنا ولا كافرًا، وله منزل بين منزلتي الإيمان والكفر، وحكمه أنه لو مات قبل التوبة خلد في النار ولم ينفعه إيمانه وطاعته وليس من الحكمة عفوه ومغفرته، وأما صاحب الصغيرة فهو عندهم مؤمن ولو اجتنب الكبائر لاستحق مغفرة الصغائر ولا يجوز الله تعذيبه عليها.

Its ruling: if he dies before repentance, he will abide eternally in the Fire, and his faith and obedience will not benefit him, and it is not from wisdom to pardon him and forgive him. As for the committer of a minor sin, he is in their view a believer, and if he avoids major sins he deserves forgiveness for minor sins, and it is not permissible that Allah punish him for them.

His ruling if he dies without repentance is eternal hellfire, and his faith does not save him. They also hold that God cannot forgive him because it would contradict wisdom. For minor sins, the person remains a believer and if he avoids major sins, he deserves forgiveness and God cannot punish him for them.

Khawarij Views on the Grave Sinner
Khawarij and their sub-sects define the grave sinner as a disbeliever or other

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Khawarij views on the grave sinner and how some early figures diverged from the majority.

Core Idea
The Khawarij were not monolithic; they held varying views on the fate of the grave sinner.

The Khawarij are often caricatured as holding that any sin equals disbelief. However, this passage from al-Nasafi shows internal diversity: some equate grave sin with polytheism, others distinguish minor from major, and still others allow temporary punishment. We also see the positions of Najda al-Hanafi, Ghilan al-Qadari, and al-Hasan al-Basri, showing that the debate extended beyond the Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Get an overall sense of the different opinions presented.
The passage lists multiple views in a compact form.
2
Identify the majority view and its variations
The majority says any sin is disbelief leading to eternal hell. Note the two variations: some say polytheism, others differentiate minor and major sins.
3
Examine Najda al-Hanafi's position
Najda al-Hanafi differs by saying the grave sinner is punished temporarily and then enters Paradise without pardon. This is a distinct view within the Khawarij.
Notice that he rejects eternal hellfire for the grave sinner.
4
Consider the views of Ghilan and al-Hasan
Ghilan al-Qadari allows pardon but with a logical restriction. Al-Hasan al-Basri considers the grave sinner a hypocrite. Reflect on how these views broaden the discussion beyond the Khawarij.
These figures are not Khawarij but weigh in on the same issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول جمهور الخوارج: إن من عصى الله تعالى فقد كفر فكان كافرًا وحكمه أنه يخلد في النار صغيرة كان من فعل أم كبيرة، ومنهم من يقول هو مشرك ومنهم من فرق بين الصغيرة وبين الكبيرة ويحكم بكفره وتخليده في النار بارتكابه الكبيرة دون الصغيرة، وحكى عن نجدة الحنفي صاحب النجدات من الخوارج أن صاحب الكبيرة لا يخلد بل يعذب لا محالة بقدر ذنبه ولا يجوز العفو عنه ثم كان عاقبة أمره دخول الجنة، وحكى عن غيلان القدري أنه كان يقول: إن العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة جائراً إلا أنه تعالى إذا عفا عن أحد ارتكب كبيرة لم يجز له تعذيب غيره على تلك الكبيرة، وحكى عن الحسن البصري كان يقول: إن صاحب الكبيرة منافق.
The saying of the majority of the Khawarij: Whoever disobeys Allah has disbelieved, so he is a disbeliever, and its ruling is that he will abide eternally in the Fire, whether the sin is minor or major. And among them are those who say he is a polytheist. And among them are those who differentiate between the minor and major sin, and rule that he is a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire for committing the major sin, not the minor. And it is narrated from Najda al-Hanafi, leader of the Najdat among the Khawarij, that the grave sinner does not abide eternally but is certainly punished according to his sin, and pardon is not permissible, then the outcome of his affair is entering Paradise. And it is narrated from Ghilan al-Qadari that he used to say: Pardon for the grave sinner is permissible, except that if Allah pardons one who committed a major sin, He is not permitted to punish another for that same sin. And it is narrated from al-Hasan al-Basri that he used to say: The grave sinner is a hypocrite.
Source pages p. 1044

Commentary
This passage presents the diverse views of the Khawarij and some early figures on the grave sinner. The majority of Khawarij hold that any sin, minor or major, constitutes disbelief and results in eternal hellfire. Some say the sinner becomes a polytheist (mushrik). Others distinguish between minor and major sins, ruling that only major sins lead to disbelief and eternity in hell. Najda al-Hanafi, a Kharijite leader, holds that the grave sinner is punished temporarily but ultimately enters Paradise without pardon. Ghilan al-Qadari allows pardon but limits it so that if God pardons one person, He cannot punish another for the same sin. Al-Hasan al-Basri is reported to have considered the grave sinner a hypocrite (munāfiq).

Key Terms
خوارج
كفر
مشرك
نجدة الحنفي
غيلان القدري
الحسن البصري
منافق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Khawarij believe that any sin leads to eternal hellfire.
Correction: The passage shows diversity: some Khawarij hold that only major sins lead to eternal hell, and Najda al-Hanafi believes the grave sinner eventually enters Paradise.

Checkpoint
Which Kharijite figure held that the grave sinner does not abide eternally in hell but is punished and then enters Paradise?
A) The majority of Khawarij
B) Najda al-Hanafi
C) Ghilan al-Qadari
D) Al-Hasan al-Basri

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij held diverse views on the grave sinner, ranging from eternal hell to temporary punishment and even pardon, showing that early Islamic theology was not monolithic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim commit a sin, do you immediately question their faith? How does the Khawarij's extreme view challenge you to check your own tendency to judge?
?
This lesson shows that even those known for harsh views (the Khawarij) had internal disagreement. In matters of religion where you feel certain, do you leave yourself open to being wrong, especially when it affects how you view others?
?
The passage records al-Hasan al-Basri saying the grave sinner is a hypocrite. When you sin, do you see yourself as a hypocrite, and what does that mean for your relationship with Allah — do you despair or take it as a call to sincerity?

The Khawarij Proof from Quran 4:14
Khawarij cite Quran 4:14 as proof for excommunication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Khawarij used Quran 4:14 as scriptural evidence for their doctrine of excommunicating all sinners.

Core Idea
One verse, one extreme conclusion: the Khawarij's takfir of all sinners.

The Khawarij are known for their extreme position that any sin, minor or major, constitutes disbelief. To support this, they turned to a specific verse from the Quran. This lesson examines how they interpreted Quran 4:14 as proof for their doctrine, and why this interpretation is contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Khawarij doctrine
Understand that the Khawarij held that any disobedience to Allah results in kufr (disbelief).
This is the extreme position that this verse is used to support.
2
Read the verse and its citation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: 'يَعْصِ' (disobey), 'حُدُودَهُ' (His limits), 'خَالِدًا' (eternally).
3
Analyze the implication
Consider why the Khawarij saw eternal punishment as evidence that the sinner is a disbeliever.
Think about the logical link between eternal punishment and disbelief in their reasoning.
4
Recognize the limitation
Reflect on how other scholars might counter this argument using other verses about forgiveness.
This verse does not explicitly say the sinner is a disbeliever; it only mentions eternal punishment.

The Status of the Major Sinner: Believer or Disbeliever?
The author argues that all sins are disobedience and the sinner belongs with the disbelievers, refuting the view that he is only a mushrik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the major sinner is categorized among the disbelievers based on Quranic divisions, and how this refutes the view that he is only a mushrik.

Core Idea
The major sinner belongs to the people of the left, not just a mushrik.

This lesson explores a key theological debate in Islamic thought: the status of someone who commits major sins. The author presents Quranic evidence to argue that such a person is counted among the disbelievers, challenging the view that they are merely polytheists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the Quranic citations and the flow of argument.
The passage uses several Quranic verses to build its case.
2
Identify the two groups of people
Find where the author divides people into the companions of the right (believers) and the companions of the left (disbelievers). Note which group the disobedient sinner is placed in.
The verse references are 84:7 and 69:25.
3
Understand the opposing view and its refutation
The passage refutes those who say the major sinner is a mushrik (polytheist) rather than a kafir. See how the author uses verse 18:110 to counter this.
The opposing view claims the sinner reached that state by action alone, not speech.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the concept of faith and sin. How does it affect one's understanding of eternal punishment?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيها من يعصيه يدخله نارا خالدًا فيها ثم قال : لَا يَصْلَنَهَا إِلَّا الْأَشْقَى الَّذِي كَذَّبَ وَتَوَلَّى والذنوب كلها في تحقيق اسم العصيان واحدة، ثم إن الله تعالى أخبر أن وقال تعالى: وَاتَّقُوا النَّارَ الَّتي أُعِدَّتْ لِلْكَفِرِينَ وقال: وَمَن لَّمْ يَحْكُم بِمَا أَنزَلَ اللهُ فأولئكَ هُمُ الكَفِرُونَ وقال: فَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابُهُ بِيَمِينِهِ وقال: وَأَمَّا مَنْ أُوتِيَ كِتَابَهُ بِشِمَالِهِ قسم الناس قسمين: أحدهما: أصحاب اليمين وهم المؤمنون، والثاني: أصحاب الشمال وهم الكافرون، والعاصي من أصحاب الشمال. والذين يقولون إنه يكون مشركاً لا كافرًا يزعمون أنه صار إلى ما صار بالفعل دون القول، وقد قال الله تعالى: فَمَن كَانَ يَرْجُو لِقَاءَ رَبِّهِ فَلْيَعْمَلْ عَمَلًا صَالِحًا وَلَا يُشْرِكْ بِعِبَادَةِ رَبِّهِ أَحَدًا
In it: whoever disobeys Him, He will cause him to enter a fire, remaining therein forever. Then He said: "None shall enter it except the most wretched, who denies and turns away." And all sins are one in the realization of the name 'disobedience.' Then God Almighty informed that... And He said: "And fear the Fire which has been prepared for the disbelievers." And He said: "And whoever does not judge by what God has revealed, then those are the disbelievers." And He said: "Then as for he who is given his book in his right hand..." And He said: "And as for he who is given his book in his left hand..." He divided people into two divisions: one: the companions of the right, and they are the believers; the second: the companions of the left, and they are the disbelievers; and the disobedient one is from the companions of the left. And those who say that he is a polytheist (mushrik), not a disbeliever, claim that he arrived at what he arrived at by action, not by speech. And God Almighty has said: "So whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord, let him do righteous work and not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord."
Source pages p. 1045

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the major sinner (sāḥib al-kabīra) is classified among the disbelievers, not merely as a polytheist (mushrik). The author begins by citing a Quranic verse (92:15-16) that limits eternal hellfire to the most wretched, which refers to deniers and those who turn away. He then states that all sins are unified under the term 'disobedience' (ʿiṣyān). Next, he cites several Quranic verses: 3:131 commands fear of the Fire prepared for disbelievers; 5:44 declares those who do not judge by God's revelation as disbelievers; and 84:7 and 69:25 refer to the division on Judgment Day between those given their book in the right hand and those given it in the left. From these, the author concludes that God divided people into two groups: the people of the right (believers) and the people of the left (disbelievers), and the disobedient sinner belongs to the latter group. The passage then refutes an opposing view: those who claim the major sinner is a polytheist (mushrik) rather than a disbeliever (kāfir) argue that he reached this state through action without verbal denial. The author counters by quoting 18:110, which warns against associating partners with God in worship, implying that such action itself constitutes shirk, but the earlier verses clearly label such people as disbelievers. Thus, the argument uses Quranic classifications to affirm that the major sinner is a disbeliever, not merely a mushrik.

Correction: The author argues that the major sinner is from the companions of the left, i.e., disbelievers, based on Quranic classifications.

Al-Hasan al-Basri's Evidence: The Major Sinner as a Hypocrite
Al-Hasan al-Basri argues that the major sinner is a hypocrite with Quran and hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Hasan al-Basri's argument that committing major sins makes one a hypocrite, based on Quranic testing and a hadith on signs of hypocrisy.

Core Idea
Actions that contradict faith are hypocrisy, even if verbally professed.

In this lesson, we explore a controversial view from al-Hasan al-Basri: that the major sinner is not merely corrupt but a true hypocrite. His argument draws on the Quranic concept of testing and a famous hadith listing three signs of hypocrisy. By analogy, he concludes that any major sin earns the same label. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core claim
Al-Hasan says that contradicting faith by action is hypocrisy. Identify how he defines nifāq in the first sentence.
Notice that he emphasizes preservation and observance of faith's boundaries.
2
Analyze the Quranic verse
Read Quran 29:1-3. Why does God test people? How does testing separate truthful from liars? Connect this to the idea that words alone are insufficient.
The verse implies that claiming faith must be proven by actions under trial.
3
Explore the hadith analogy
The Prophet lists three signs of a hypocrite. Al-Hasan argues that other sins equal these three in being disobedience, so they equally deserve the name 'hypocrite'. Consider the principle of analogy (qiyās).
The key is that the three signs are acts of disobedience, and any major sin shares that property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه قول الحسن أنه خالف بفعله ما أعطى بلسانه من الإيمان وحفظ حدوده، وتعاهد ذلك وهذا هو النفاق. قال الله تعالى : الر أَحَسِبَ النَّاسُ أَن يُتْرَكُوا أَن يَقُولُوا آمَنَّا وَهُمْ لَا يُفْتَنُونَ وَلَقَدْ فَتَنَّا الَّذِينَ مِن قَبْلِهِمْ فَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ صَدَقُوا وَلَيَعْلَمَنَّ الْكَاذِبِينَ ويدل عليه ما روى عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : علامة المنافق ثلاث إذا ائتمن خان وإذا حدث كذب وإذا وعد أخلف وغير هذه الأشياء الثلاثة يساويها في كونها عصيانا فيساويها في إيجاب اسم النفاق وحكمه لمرتكبها.
And the meaning of al-Hasan's statement: he contradicted by his action what he gave with his tongue of faith and its preservation and observance, and that is hypocrisy (nifāq). God Almighty said: "Alif Lām Rā. Do the people think that they will be left to say, 'We believe,' while they are not tested? And We certainly tested those before them, and God will surely know those who are truthful and will surely know the liars." And it is indicated by what was narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that he said: "The sign of the hypocrite is three: when he is entrusted he betrays, when he speaks he lies, and when he promises he breaks." And other than these three things equal them in being disobedience, so they equal them in imposing the name of hypocrisy and its ruling on the one who commits them.
Source pages p. 1045

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence (dalīl) of al-Hasan al-Basrī for his view that the major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) is a hypocrite (munāfiq). The argument is structured as follows: First, al-Hasan states his core reasoning: the major sinner contradicts by his action the faith he verbally professes and the boundaries he maintained, and this contradiction is precisely hypocrisy (nifāq). To support this, he quotes Quran 29:1-3, which challenges those who claim faith without being tested; God tests people to distinguish the truthful from the liars. This implies that saying 'we believe' is not sufficient; actions must confirm it. Then he cites a well-known hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) listing three signs of the hypocrite: betrayal of trust, lying in speech, and breaking promises. Al-Hasan argues that other sins (like those of the major sinner) are equal to these three in being acts of disobedience (ʿiṣyān). Therefore, by analogy, committing any major sin equally warrants the name and ruling of hypocrisy. In other words, if betrayal, lying, and promise-breaking earn the label 'hypocrite' because they are acts of disobedience, then any major sin does the same. Thus, the major sinner is not simply a fāsiq (corrupt) but a munāfiq.

العصيان
العلامات
الإيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hypocrisy is only about belief, not actions.
Correction: Al-Hasan shows that action contradicting profession of faith is itself hypocrisy, based on the Quranic test and prophetic tradition.

Checkpoint

B) Contradicting one's faith by actions
C) Not performing religious duties

Key Takeaway
Al-Hasan al-Basri argues that any major sin constitutes hypocrisy because it contradicts the verbal profession of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The hadith mentions three signs of hypocrisy. Honestly examine your life: in which of these areas are you most prone to fail? What concrete steps will you take this week to align your behavior with your faith?

The Mu'tazila on the Name of the Major Sinner
The Mu'tazila enumerate the three views on naming the major sinner and justify their intermediate position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning for calling the major sinner a fasiq and not a believer, disbeliever, or hypocrite.

Core Idea
The name 'fasiq' is the only agreed-upon name for the major sinner.

The Mu'tazila began their argument by surveying the existing opinions on how to name a person who commits a major sin. They noticed that all groups agreed on calling him a fasiq, but disagreed on other names. Seizing this consensus, they argued that the major sinner should be called only by the name on which everyone agrees—fasiq—and not by the disputed good names like 'believer'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on naming
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the three opinions listed and how the Mu'tazila derive their own position.
Pay attention to the phrase 'so we took what they agreed upon'.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three groups mentioned: the Jamā'a, the Khawārij, and al-Hasan al-Baṣrī with his followers. Write down what each group says the major sinner is.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning
The Mu'tazila note that the ummah agreed on 'fasiq' but differed on others. They adopt the agreed-upon name alone. Also, note the argument about 'ugly names' and 'good names' to exclude 'believer'.
Believer is a good name; the major sinner deserves only ugly names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة يزعمون في الاسم أن الناس اختلفوا في صاحب الكبيرة على أقوال منهم من قال : إنه مؤمن بما معه من التصديق فاسق بما اقترف من الذنب وهو قول الجماعة، ومنهم من قال : إنه كافر وهو فاسق وهو قول الخوارج، ومنهم من قال هو منافق فاسق وهو قول الحسن ومن تابعه، فاتفقت الأمة على إطلاق اسم الفاسق واختلفوا في إطلاق اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق، فأخذنا نحن بما اتفقوا عليه وهو اسم الفاسق وتركنا ما اختلفوا فيه قلنا: إنه فاسق وليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ولا منافق ولأنه لما سمي بالأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والجائر والفاجر والظالم لا يسمى بالأسماء الطيبة، والمؤمن من الأسماء الطيبة، وأما حكم الخلود فلقوله تعالى: وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا الآية، وقوله: إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوَالَ الْيَتَامَى ظُلْمًا الآية، وقال تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقًا فالله تعالى جعل الفسق بمقابلة الإيمان وجعل المؤمن قسمًا والفاسق قسما، دل أن الفاسق غير والمؤمن غير، ثم بين حكم كل واحد منهما فقال: أَمَّا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ فَلَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْمَأْوَى نُزُلًا بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ فَسَقُوا فَمَأْوَاهُمُ النَّارُ كُلَّمَا أَرَادُوا أَن يَخْرُجُوا مِنْهَا أُعِيدُوا فِيهَا وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُمْ بِهِ تُكَذِّبُونَ فكان في الآية دليل انتفاء اسم الإيمان وثبوت اسم الفسق ودليل حكم التخليد، ثم إنهم يقولون: إن الإخبار بتعذيب القاتل للمؤمن عمدًا وغير ذلك قد ورد، ولا وجه للقول بالخلف الوعيد لما فيه من إثبات الكذب ولا وجه إلى القول بتخصيص عموم الأخبار
And the Mu'tazila claim regarding the name (al-ism) that people differed concerning the committer of the major sin (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) on several opinions: among them, one who said: he is a believer (mu'min) because of the affirmation (taṣdīq) he has, but a transgressor (fāsiq) because of the sin he committed—this is the view of the majority (jamā'a); and among them, one who said: he is a disbeliever (kāfir) and a transgressor—this is the view of the Khawārij; and among them, one who said: he is a hypocrite (munāfiq) and a transgressor—this is the view of al-Hasan and those who followed him. So the ummah agreed on applying the name 'transgressor' (fāsiq) and differed on applying the name 'believer,' 'disbeliever,' and 'hypocrite.' So we (the Mu'tazila) took what they agreed upon, which is the name 'transgressor,' and left aside what they differed on. We said: he is a transgressor, not a believer, nor a disbeliever, nor a hypocrite. And because when he is called by the ugly names like 'transgressor,' 'unjust,' 'immoral,' 'oppressor,' he should not be called by the good names; and 'believer' is among the good names. As for the ruling of eternal staying (khulūd), it is due to His statement: "And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever" (4:93), and His statement: "Indeed, those who devour the property of orphans unjustly..." (4:10), and He said: "Is he who is a believer like him who is a transgressor? They are not equal" (32:18). So God Almighty made transgression (fisq) opposite to faith (īmān) and made the believer a category and the transgressor a category, indicating that the transgressor is other than the believer. Then He explained the judgment of each of them, saying: "As for those who believe and do righteous deeds, for them are gardens of refuge as accommodation for what they used to do. And as for those who transgress (fasaqū), their refuge is the Fire; whenever they desire to come out of it, they are returned therein, and it is said to them, 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny.'" So in this verse there is evidence of the negation of the name 'belief' and the affirmation of the name 'transgression' and evidence of the judgment of eternal staying. Then they say: the reports of punishing the intentional murderer of a believer and other things have come, and there is no way to say that the threat is not carried out (khulf al-wa'īd) because that would involve affirming lying, and there is no way to say that the general reports are particularized.
Source pages p. 1046

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's argument for their distinctive position on the major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra), which is the 'intermediate position' (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). The argument is divided into two parts: the name (al-ism) and the judgment (al-ḥukm) of eternal punishment (al-khulūd). In the first part, the Mu'tazila review the three existing views on naming: (1) the majority (Jamā'a) say the major sinner is a believer due to his affirmation but a fāsiq due to his sin; (2) the Khawārij say he is a kāfir; (3) al-Hasan al-Baṣrī and his followers say he is a munāfiq. The Mu'tazila note that all groups agree on calling him a fāsiq (transgressor), but differ on the other names. Therefore, the Mu'tazila adopt the name on which there is consensus—fāsiq—and reject the disputed names. They further argue that since he is called by ugly names like fāsiq, jā'ir (unjust), fājir (immoral), and ẓālim (oppressor), he should not be called by good names; and 'believer' (mu'min) is a good name. Thus, he is neither a believer, nor a disbeliever, nor a hypocrite, but a fāsiq. The second part deals with eternal punishment (khulūd). The Mu'tazila cite two Quranic verses: 4:93, which threatens the intentional murderer of a believer with eternal hellfire; and 4:10, which threatens those who devour orphans' property (implying eternal punishment). They then cite 32:18-20, which explicitly contrasts believers and fāsiqs: believers get gardens, while fāsiqs get the Fire where they are repeatedly returned if they try to leave. The Mu'tazila argue that this verse proves that the name 'belief' is negated for the fāsiq and that the fāsiq belongs to the Fire forever. Finally, they reject two potential counterarguments: (a) that the threat might not be fulfilled (khulf al-wa'īd) because that would imply God breaking His promise, i.e., lying; (b) that the general threats (e.g., against murderers) might be particularized to exclude some cases. Both are dismissed as invalid.

Key Terms
الفاسق
صاحب الكبيرة
الاسم
المنزلة بين المنزلتين
الخوارج
الحسن البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that the major sinner has any faith (īmān).
Correction: The Mu'tazila affirm that the major sinner has taṣdīq (affirmation) but do not call him a mu'min because he commits major sins. They argue that the name 'believer' is a good name and does not apply to one who deserves ugly names.

Checkpoint
What name do the Mu'tazila use for the major sinner, based on consensus among all groups?
A) Believer (mu'min)
B) Disbeliever (kāfir)
C) Hypocrite (munāfiq)
D) Transgressor (fāsiq)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that the only agreed-upon name for a major sinner is fāsiq; he is neither a believer, disbeliever, nor hypocrite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, what name do you instinctively label them with in your heart? Are you quick to call them 'not a believer' or do you still see their faith? How does this lesson challenge the way you judge others?
?
The Mu'tazila insisted on using only the agreed-upon name 'fāsiq' to avoid division. In your own relationships, where do you insist on labels that cause disunity rather than focusing on what is certain? Will you commit to using softer, more certain language about others from today?
?
If you were the major sinner described in this passage, what name would you want God and people to call you? Could you accept being called only 'transgressor' and still hope for mercy? Reflect on whether you rely on the name 'believer' as a sense of security or as a call to action.

The Mu'tazila's Proof for Eternal Punishment of the Major Sinner
The Mu'tazila present Quranic evidence for the eternal punishment of the major sinner and reject counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic verses the Mu'tazila use to argue for the eternal punishment of the major sinner and how they defend this view.

Core Idea
Quranic verses explicitly threaten eternal punishment for major sinners.

After establishing the name, the Mu'tazila turn to the ruling of the major sinner: eternal punishment in hell. They support this with direct Quranic evidence, particularly verses that link sin to eternal damnation. They also anticipate and reject two counterarguments—that the threat might not be carried out or that it might be particularized—showing the strength of their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on khulūd
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three Quranic verses cited and the two rebuttals at the end.
The verses are 4:93, 4:10, and 32:18-20.
2
Analyze each verse
For each verse, identify how it is used to support eternal punishment. Verse 4:93 explicitly mentions 'abiding therein forever' for intentional murder of a believer. Verse 4:10 threatens those who devour orphans' property (a major sin). Verse 32:18-20 contrasts believers and fāsiqs, giving gardens to believers and the Fire to fāsiqs, with no exit.
3
Understand the rebuttals
The Mu'tazila reject the idea that the threat might not be carried out (khulf al-wa'īd) because that would imply God is lying, which is impossible. They also reject the idea that the general threats are particularized (takhṣīṣ) without warrant.
These rebuttals are important to defend the literal reading of the verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتزلة يزعمون في الاسم أن الناس اختلفوا في صاحب الكبيرة على أقوال منهم من قال : إنه مؤمن بما معه من التصديق فاسق بما اقترف من الذنب وهو قول الجماعة، ومنهم من قال : إنه كافر وهو فاسق وهو قول الخوارج، ومنهم من قال هو منافق فاسق وهو قول الحسن ومن تابعه، فاتفقت الأمة على إطلاق اسم الفاسق واختلفوا في إطلاق اسم المؤمن والكافر والمنافق، فأخذنا نحن بما اتفقوا عليه وهو اسم الفاسق وتركنا ما اختلفوا فيه قلنا: إنه فاسق وليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ولا منافق ولأنه لما سمي بالأسماء الخبيثة كالفاسق والجائر والفاجر والظالم لا يسمى بالأسماء الطيبة، والمؤمن من الأسماء الطيبة، وأما حكم الخلود فلقوله تعالى: وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَالِدًا فِيهَا الآية، وقوله: إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَأْكُلُونَ أَمْوَالَ الْيَتَامَى ظُلْمًا الآية، وقال تعالى: أَفَمَن كَانَ مُؤْمِنًا كَمَن كَانَ فَاسِقًا فالله تعالى جعل الفسق بمقابلة الإيمان وجعل المؤمن قسمًا والفاسق قسما، دل أن الفاسق غير والمؤمن غير، ثم بين حكم كل واحد منهما فقال: أَمَّا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ فَلَهُمْ جَنَّاتُ الْمَأْوَى نُزُلًا بِمَا كَانُوا يَعْمَلُونَ وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ فَسَقُوا فَمَأْوَاهُمُ النَّارُ كُلَّمَا أَرَادُوا أَن يَخْرُجُوا مِنْهَا أُعِيدُوا فِيهَا وَقِيلَ لَهُمْ ذُوقُوا عَذَابَ النَّارِ الَّذِي كُنتُمْ بِهِ تُكَذِّبُونَ فكان في الآية دليل انتفاء اسم الإيمان وثبوت اسم الفسق ودليل حكم التخليد، ثم إنهم يقولون: إن الإخبار بتعذيب القاتل للمؤمن عمدًا وغير ذلك قد ورد، ولا وجه للقول بالخلف الوعيد لما فيه من إثبات الكذب ولا وجه إلى القول بتخصيص عموم الأخبار
And the Mu'tazila claim regarding the name (al-ism) that people differed concerning the committer of the major sin (ṣāḥib al-kabīra) on several opinions: among them, one who said: he is a believer (mu'min) because of the affirmation (taṣdīq) he has, but a transgressor (fāsiq) because of the sin he committed—this is the view of the majority (jamā'a); and among them, one who said: he is a disbeliever (kāfir) and a transgressor—this is the view of the Khawārij; and among them, one who said: he is a hypocrite (munāfiq) and a transgressor—this is the view of al-Hasan and those who followed him. So the ummah agreed on applying the name 'transgressor' (fāsiq) and differed on applying the name 'believer,' 'disbeliever,' and 'hypocrite.' So we (the Mu'tazila) took what they agreed upon, which is the name 'transgressor,' and left aside what they differed on. We said: he is a transgressor, not a believer, nor a disbeliever, nor a hypocrite. And because when he is called by the ugly names like 'transgressor,' 'unjust,' 'immoral,' 'oppressor,' he should not be called by the good names; and 'believer' is among the good names. As for the ruling of eternal staying (khulūd), it is due to His statement: "And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever" (4:93), and His statement: "Indeed, those who devour the property of orphans unjustly..." (4:10), and He said: "Is he who is a believer like him who is a transgressor? They are not equal" (32:18). So God Almighty made transgression (fisq) opposite to faith (īmān) and made the believer a category and the transgressor a category, indicating that the transgressor is other than the believer. Then He explained the judgment of each of them, saying: "As for those who believe and do righteous deeds, for them are gardens of refuge as accommodation for what they used to do. And as for those who transgress (fasaqū), their refuge is the Fire; whenever they desire to come out of it, they are returned therein, and it is said to them, 'Taste the punishment of the Fire which you used to deny.'" So in this verse there is evidence of the negation of the name 'belief' and the affirmation of the name 'transgression' and evidence of the judgment of eternal staying. Then they say: the reports of punishing the intentional murderer of a believer and other things have come, and there is no way to say that the threat is not carried out (khulf al-wa'īd) because that would involve affirming lying, and there is no way to say that the general reports are particularized.
Source pages p. 1046

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's argument for their distinctive position on the major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra), which is the 'intermediate position' (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn). The argument is divided into two parts: the name (al-ism) and the judgment (al-ḥukm) of eternal punishment (al-khulūd). In the first part, the Mu'tazila review the three existing views on naming: (1) the majority (Jamā'a) say the major sinner is a believer due to his affirmation but a fāsiq due to his sin; (2) the Khawārij say he is a kāfir; (3) al-Hasan al-Baṣrī and his followers say he is a munāfiq. The Mu'tazila note that all groups agree on calling him a fāsiq (transgressor), but differ on the other names. Therefore, the Mu'tazila adopt the name on which there is consensus—fāsiq—and reject the disputed names. They further argue that since he is called by ugly names like fāsiq, jā'ir (unjust), fājir (immoral), and ẓālim (oppressor), he should not be called by good names; and 'believer' (mu'min) is a good name. Thus, he is neither a believer, nor a disbeliever, nor a hypocrite, but a fāsiq. The second part deals with eternal punishment (khulūd). The Mu'tazila cite two Quranic verses: 4:93, which threatens the intentional murderer of a believer with eternal hellfire; and 4:10, which threatens those who devour orphans' property (implying eternal punishment). They then cite 32:18-20, which explicitly contrasts believers and fāsiqs: believers get gardens, while fāsiqs get the Fire where they are repeatedly returned if they try to leave. The Mu'tazila argue that this verse proves that the name 'belief' is negated for the fāsiq and that the fāsiq belongs to the Fire forever. Both are dismissed as invalid.

?

General Commands and Their Abrogation
The author argues that general commands without specification cannot be abrogated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a general command without specification cannot be abrogated by specification, but only by explicit abrogation.

Core Idea
General commands are binding as stated; any limitation is abrogation.

The author begins by establishing a key principle of Quranic interpretation: when God issues a general command without qualification, it applies universally. To interpret it as limited would effectively change the command, which is not permissible for divine speech. This principle underpins the discussion of divine threats and forgiveness that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the first part of the source passage, which covers the logical argument about general commands and abrogation. Pay attention to the technical terms.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the argument: (a) a general command without specification indicates generality, (b) it is as if each individual is mentioned by name, (c) therefore specifying it would be abrogation, (d) abrogation of divine reports is false.
Notice the author uses 'fasar' (so it becomes) to link steps.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this principle matters: it prevents arbitrary limitation of Quranic commands and threats. The author uses this to argue that the threat of punishment for major sins cannot be simply explained away.
This connects to the later discussion on forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجوب القول بالعموم المتعرى عن دليل الخصوص إذ الصيغة المتعرية عن دليل الخصوص دليل إرادة المتكلم العموم، فصار كأنه ذكر كل فرد من أفراد العموم باسمه الخاص، وما هذا سبيله لا يتصور تخصيصه بل يكون إخراجه على حكم العموم نسخا، ولا وجه إلى القول بنسخ الأخبار لما فيه من إثبات الكذب، وجماعة منهم يجوزون العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة في الحكمة إلا أنهم يقولون: بتحقيق الوعيد للأخبار الواردة في الكتاب بتعذيبه وجماعة منهم يقولون ليس في الحكمة جواز عفوه إذ لو كان يجوز عفوه لما جاز تعذيبه إذ العفو حينئذ يكون كرما، والإعراض عن الكرم غير جائز في الحكمة، وهذا منهم قريب من تعليل إيجاب الصلح بل هو عينه، وأما الصغائر فإنها مغفورة عند اجتناب الكبائر لقوله تعالى: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه نكفر عنكم سيئاتكم الآية.
For the obligatoriness of stating the general [command] stripped of evidence of specification, since the formulation stripped of evidence of specification is evidence of the speaker's intention of generality. So it becomes as if he mentioned every individual of the generality by its specific name. And whatever is of this way cannot be conceived to be specified; rather, its removal from the ruling of generality would be abrogation. And there is no way to say that reports (akhbar) can be abrogated because that would entail establishing falsehood. And a group among them (the Mu'tazila) permit forgiveness of the committer of a major sin in [divine] wisdom, except that they say: the threat (al-wa'id) is realized [i.e., must be carried out] due to the reports in the Book about his punishment. And another group among them say: in wisdom there is no permissibility of forgiving him, for if it were permissible to forgive him, it would not be permissible to punish him, since forgiveness then would be generosity (karam), and turning away from generosity is not permissible in wisdom. And this from them is close to the reasoning for obligating reconciliation (sulh); rather, it is the same. As for minor sins, they are forgiven when major sins are avoided, due to His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins that you are forbidden, We will expiate for you your [minor] sins' (Quran 4:31).
Source pages p. 1047

Commentary
This passage combines a logical argument about general commands and a discussion of the Mu'tazila positions on forgiveness of major sins, followed by a Quranic proof for forgiveness of minor sins. The author first argues that a general statement without specification indicates generality and cannot be abrogated except by explicit abrogation, otherwise it would imply falsehood. This principle seems to be applied to the Quranic threats. Then he presents two Mu'tazila views: some allow forgiveness of major sinners but insist that the threat of punishment must be fulfilled based on Quranic verses; others deny that forgiveness is permissible because it would contradict wisdom, as forgiveness is generous and wisdom cannot turn away from generosity. He equates the latter with obligating reconciliation (sulh), implying it is unreasonable. Then he turns to minor sins, affirming that they are forgiven when one avoids major sins, citing Quran 4:31 as proof. This establishes the Sunni position: major sins are not necessarily forgiven without repentance, but minor sins are forgiven if major sins are avoided.

Key Terms
عموم
تخصيص
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A general command can always be specified or limited without changing its essence.
Correction: Specifying a general command without evidence effectively abrogates it, which is not permissible for divine commands.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about a general command that has no evidence of specification?
A) It must be limited to a specific meaning.
B) It indicates the speaker intends generality.
C) It is automatically abrogated.
D) It is ambiguous and requires interpretation.

Key Takeaway
A general command without specification is binding as stated; any attempt to limit it is considered abrogation, which is impermissible for divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear command from Allah—like prayer or honesty—as outdated or only for certain people? How does the principle that a general command is as binding as if your name were mentioned challenge that attitude? What will you do differently today to obey a general command you previously overlooked?
?
When you read a verse like 'O you who believe, fulfill contracts' (5:1), do you immediately think of exceptions without evidence? According to this lesson, what is the danger of treating such commands as negotiable? How will you approach the next general command you encounter in the Quran?
?
If every general command stripped of specification is unchangeable unless abrogated by another revelation, what does that mean for your tendency to rationalize disobedience? Are you willing to submit to the literal scope of Allah's commands, even when it is difficult? What step will you take to act on one such command this week?

Forgiveness of Major and Minor Sins
The author discusses Mu'tazila views on forgiveness of major sins and affirms forgiveness of minor sins from Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila positions on forgiveness of major sins and the Quranic basis for forgiveness of minor sins.

Core Idea
Minor sins are forgiven if major sins are avoided; major sin forgiveness is debated.

After establishing the nature of general commands, the author now tackles the question of divine forgiveness. He presents two Mu'tazila views: some allow forgiveness of major sinners but insist on punishment, others deny it as contrary to wisdom. He then affirms the Sunni position that minor sins are forgiven when major sins are shunned, citing Quran 4:31.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Mu'tazila views
Read the part of the source that discusses the two Mu'tazila positions on forgiveness of major sinners. Note the reasoning behind each view.
The first group says the threat is realized; the second says forgiveness is not permissible in wisdom.
2
Understand the reasoning of each group
The first group allows forgiveness but insists the threat of punishment must be carried out due to Quranic reports. The second argues that if forgiveness were possible, punishment would not be permissible because forgiveness is generous and wisdom cannot turn away from generosity.
The author equates the second view with obligating reconciliation (sulh), implying it is flawed.
3
Examine the Quranic proof for minor sins
The author cites Quran 4:31 to affirm that minor sins are forgiven when major sins are avoided. Reflect on how this establishes the Sunni position.
This is a direct scriptural proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must punish every sin without exception.
Correction: The passage shows that there are differing views on whether major sins can be forgiven, and that minor sins are forgiven when major sins are avoided (Quran 4:31).

Minor sins are forgiven when major sins are avoided, as per Quran 4:31; major sin forgiveness is a matter of debate among Mu'tazila.
